# TO THE KING.

TO THE

K I N G.

PRESUME to lay before Your Majesty the Hiflory of a

Period, which, if the abilities of the Writer were equal to
the dignityof the Subjet, would not be unworthy the at-
tention of a Monarch, who is no less a Judge than a Patron
of Literary Merit.

HISTORY claims it as her prerogative to offer inftruc-

tion to KINGS, as well as to their people. What reflec-
tions the Reign of the Emperor CHARLES V. may suggest
to Your Majesty, it becomes not me to conjecture. But
your Subjecs cannot observe the various calamities, which
that Monarch's ambition to be diftinguilhed as a Conquer-
or, brought upon his dominibns, without recollecing the
fclic'ty of their own times, and looking up with gratitude to
thir Sovereign, who, during the fervour of youth. and a-
nlidt the career of vi&ory, possessed such felf-command,
and maturity of judgment, as to set bounds to his own tri-
umphs, and prefer tte blessings of peace to the splendour of
military glory.

POSTERITY will not only celebrate the wisdom of Your
lajefty's choice, but will enumerate the many virtues, which
render Your reign conspicuous for a sacred regard to all the
duties incumbent on the Sovereign of a Free People.

IT is our happiness to feel the influence of these Virtues;

and to live under the dominion of a Prince, who delights
more in promoting the Public Welfare, than in receiving
thejuil Praise of his Royal Beneficence. I am,

SIR,
YoUR MAJESTY'S

Mosl faithful Subjet,

And molt dutiful Servant,

WILLIAM ROBERTSON.

Two great revolutions have happened in the political Rate,
and in the manners of the European nations. The firit
was occasioned by the progress of the Roman power; the
second by the subversion of it. When the spirit of conquest
led the armies of Rome beyond the Alps, they found all the
countries which they invaded, inhabited by people whom
they denominated barbarians, but who were nevertheless
brave and independent. Thef defended their ancient pof-
effions with-obftinat valour. It was by the superiority of
their discipline, rather than that of their courage, that the
Romans gained any advantage over them. A single battle
did not, as among the effeminate inhabitants of Asia, decide
the fate of a slate. The vanquished people resumed their
arms with fresh spirit, and their undisciplined valour, animated
by the 'ove of liberty, supplied the want of condu& as well
as of union. During those long and fierce struggles for do-
jminon or independence, the countries of Europe were fuc-

ceffively laid waste, a great part of their inhabitants peried
in the field, many were carried into lavery, and a feeble rem-
nant, incapable of further resistance, submitted to the Ro-
bnan power.

The Romans having thtts desolated Europe, set themselves

to civilize it. The form of government which they efta-
blifhed in the conquered provinces, though severe, was regu-
lar, and preserved public tranquillity. As a consolation for
the loss of liberty, they comrnunicated their arts, sciences,
language, and manners, to their new fubjeca. Europe be-
gan to breathe, and to recover strength after the calamities
which it had undergonel agriculture Wm encouraged; po.
pulation increased; theruined. citiei were rebuilt- newtowns
were founded; an appearance of prosperity succeeded, and
repaired, in some degree, the havock of war.

This iate, hvwever, was far frout bii happy or taour-

able to the improvement of the huma miald. The tab-
quilhed nations were difanmed by their connuewn, and o.
verawed by soldier kept in pay to reirain them. They
were given up as a prey to rapacioo goveror, who plun.

by exorbitant taxes, levied wt o little attention to the situ-

They were deprived of their ioft terprizing cititms, who
rcforted to a distant capital in i t of ldpraef nt, or of

they could retai- vigour orgcoeofity of mid. The mar-

hlabit but even the capacity of deciding for tbemfelves, or of

niofs of the Romans, like that of all great empires, degraded
and debased the human species [A].

A society in such a Rate could not subsist long. There

were defees in the Roman government, even in its most per.
fee form, which threatened its dissolution. Time ripened
these original feeds of corruption, and gave birth to many
new disorders. A constitution, unsound, and worn out,
mufr have fallen into pieces of itself, without any external
shock. The violent irruption of the Goths, Vandals, Huns,
and other barbarians, hastened this event, and precipitated
the downfal of the empire. New nations seemed to arise'
and to rui from unknown regions, in order to take ven-
geance on the Romans for the calamities which they had
in.ficted on mankind. These fierce tribes either inhabited
the various rjrovinces in Germany which had never been
subdued by the Romans, or were scattered over those valt
countries in the north of Europe, and north-west of Asia,
which are now occupied by the Danes, the Swedes, the Poles,
the fubjets of the Ruffian empire, and the Tartars. Their
condition and tranfafions, previous to their invasion of the

with rcfped to these is derived from the Romans; and as

perfet. The rude inhabitants themselves, destitute of fci-
ence as well as of records, and without leisure or curiosity
to inquire into remote events, retained, perhaps, some indif-
tina memory of recent occurrences, but beyond these, all
was buried in oblivion, or involved in darkness and in fable
[B].

The prodigious swarms which poured in upon the em.

pire from the beginning of the fourth century to the final
extinction of the Roman power, have given-rife to an opini-
on that the countries whence they issued were crowded with

inhabitants; and various theories have been formed to account

thata great part of these was covered with woods and marshes;
that some of the most considerable of the barbarous nations
subsisted entirely by hunting or pasturage, in both which
itates of society large tra&s of land are required for main-

gers to the arts and industry, without which population can-
not increase to any great degree, we must conclude, that
these countries could not be so populous in ancient times as
they are in the present, when-they fill continue to be less
peopled than any other part of Europe or of Asia.

But the fame circumitances that prevented the barbarous

nations from becoming populous, contributed'to inspire, or
to strengthen, the martial spirit by which they were diftin-
guifhed. Inured by the rigour of their climate, or the po-
verty of their foil, to hardships which rendered their bodies
firm and their minds vigorous; accustomed to a course of

they undertook, and prosecuted their military. enterprizes

ny idea [C].

Their firt inroads into the empire proceeded r4ther from

to the iword; carried off the most valuable effets of the in-

wafted all before them with fire or fwordi and: returned in

Many concurrig causes prepared the way for this grea

relaxed. The armies of the empire in the fourth and fiftl

centuries bore scarcely any resemblance to thof invincible le-
gions which had been vicorious wherever they matched.
Instead of freemen, who voluntarily took arms from the love
of glory, or of their country, provincials and barbarians were
bribed or forced into service. These were too feeble, or too
proud to submit to the fatigue of military duty. They e-
ven complained of the weight of their defensive armour as
intolerable, and laid it aside. Infantry, from which the ar-
mies of ancient Rome derived their vigour and liability, fell
into contempt; the effeminate and undisciplined soldiers of
later times could hardly be brought to venture into the field
but on horseback. These wretched troops, however, were
the only guardians of the empire. Thejealoufy of despotism
had deprived the people of the use of arms; and subje&s, op-
pressed and rendered incapable of defending themselves, had
neither spirit nor inclination to resist their invaders, from
whom they had little to fear, because their condition could
hardly be rendered more unhappy. At the fame time that
the martial spirit became extinc, the revenues of the empire
gradually diminilhed. The taste for the luxuries of the East
increased to such a pitch in the Imperial court, that great
tums were carried into India, from which, in the channel of
commerce, money never returns. By the large subsidies paid
to the barbarous nations, a ltin greater quantity of specie was
withdrawn from circulation. The frontier provinces, watled
by frequent incursions, became unable to pay the customary
tribute; and the wealth of the world, which had long center-
ed in the capital of the empire ceased to flow thither in the
fame abundance, or was diverted into other channels. The
limits of the empire continued to be as extensive as ever, while
the spirit requisite for its defence declined, and its f-efourcee
were exhausted. A vast body, languid and almost unanimat--
ed, became incapable of any effort to save itself, and was ea-
fily overpowered. The emperors, who had the absolute di-
refion of this disordered system, funk in the softness of Eaift
ern luxury, ihut up within the walls of a palace, ignorant of

Vol. I. B

war, unacquainted with affairs, and governed entirely by wos
men and eunuchs, or by miniftcrs equally effeminate, trem-
bled at the approach of danger, and, under circumitances
awhich called for the utmost vigour in council as well as in
ation, discovered all the impotent irresolution of fear and
of folly.

In every refpe& the condition of the barbarous nations

was the reverse of that of the Romans. Among the former,
the martial spirit was in full vigour; their leaders were har-
dy and enterprising; the arts which had enervated the Ro-
mans were unknown; and such was the nature of their mili-
tary institutions, that they brought forces into the field with-
out any trouble, and supported them at little expence. The
Tnercenary and effeminate troops fRationed on the frontier, a-
AIonifhed at their fierceness, either fled at their approach, or
were routed on the first onset. The feeble expedient to
which the emperors had recourse, of taking large bodies of
the barbarians into pay, and of employing them to repel new
invaders, instead of retarding, haRfened the deftruifion of the
empire. These mercenaries soon turned their anus against
their mailers, and with greater advantage than ever, for, by
serving in the Roman armies, they had acquired all the difei-
pline, or ikill in war, which the Romans still retained; and,
upon adding these to their native ferocity, they became al-
together irrefiftable.

But though from these, and many other causes, the pro-

grefs and conquests of the nations which over-ran the empire
became so extremely rapid, they were accompanied with
horrible devaalations, and an incredible deltruction of the
human species. Civilized nations, which take arms upon
cool reflection, from motives of policy or prudence, with a
view to guard against some dillant danger, or to prevent
some remote contingency, carry on their hostilities with so
little rancour or animosity, that war among them is disarmed
of half its terrors. Barbarians are firangers to such refine-
tacuait. They rush into war with impetuosity, and prosecute

it with violence. Their sole objet is to make their enemies
feel the weight of their vengeance; nor does their rage sub-
fide until it be satiated with inflicing on them every pofli-
bie calamity. It is with such a spirit that the savage tribes
in America carry on their petty wars. It was with the fame
spirit that the more powerful and no less fierce barbarians in
the north of Europe, and of Asia, fell upon the Roman em-
pire.

Wherever they marched, their rout was marked with

blood. They ravaged or deflroyed all around them. They.
made no dillin&iion between what was sacred and what was
profane. They refpeeted no age, or sex, or rank. What
escaped the fury of the firfl inundation, perished in those
which followed it. The molt fertile and populous provin-
ces were converted into deserts, in which were scattered the
ruins of v.llages and cities, that afforded Ihelter to a few mi-
ferable inhabitants whom chance had preserved, or the sword
of the enemy, wearied with deitroying, had spared. The
conquerors who firit fettled in the countries which they had
wafted, were expelled or exterminated by new invaders, who,
coming from regionsfarther removed from the civilized parts
of the world, were itill more fierce and rapacious. This
brought fresh calamities upon mankind, which did not cease
until the north, by pouring forth successive swarms, was
drained of people, and could no longer furnish instruments of
deftru&ion. Famine and pettilence, which always march in
the train of war, when it ravages with such inconsiderate cru-
elty, raged in every part of Europe, and completed its sus-
ferings. If a man were called to fix upon the period in the
history of the world, during which the condition of the hu-
man race was most calamitous and afflited, he would, with-
out hesitation, name that which elapsed from the death of
Theodosius the Great, to the establishment of the Lombards
in Italyt. The contemporary authors, who beheld that

t TheodofiCs died A. D. 39, the reign ofAlhoinus in Lonibar

scene of desolation, labour and are at a loss for expressions to

But no erprefiions can convey so perse& an idea of the

decfrative progress of the barbarians as that which must
:trike an attentive observer when he contemplates the total
change which he will discover in the Rate of Europe, after it
began to recover some degree of tranquillity, towards the
cdole of the filth century. The Saxons were by that time
mailers of the southern and more fertile provinces of Britain;
the Franks of Gaul; the Huns of Pannonia; the Goths of
Spain; the Goths and Lombards of Ital7 and the adjacent
province. Very faint vefliges of the Roman policy, jurif-
prudence, arts, or literature remained. New forms of go-
vernment, new laws, new manners, new dresses, new languages,
and new names of men and countries, were every where in-
troduced. To make a great or sudden alteration with re-
fpea to any of theie, uncls where the ancient inhabitants of
a country have been almost totally exterminated, has proved
an undertaking beyond the power of the greatest conqueror*'
[D]. The great changewhich the settlement ofthebarba-
rous nations occasioned in the state of Europe, may therefore.
be confidered as a more decisive proof than even the tefimo-
ny of contemporary hiltorians, of the deftruiwe violence
with which these invaders carried on their conqeftsi andiof
the havock which they had made from one extremity of this

In the obscurity of the chaos occasioned by this general

wreck of nations, we must fcarch for the feeds of order, and
endeavour to discover the firit rudiments of the policy and

[D] Note IV. [E] Note V.

laws now eltabliflied in Europe. To this source the hiftori-
ans of its different kingdoms have attempted, though with
less attention and industry than the importance of the enqui-
ry merits, to trace back the institutions and customs peculiar
to their countrymen. It is not my province to give a mi-
nute detail of the progress of government and manners in
each particular nation, whose tranfa&ions are the objec of
the following history. But, in order to exhibit a just view
of the fate of Europe at the opening of the sixteenth cen.
tury, it is necessary to look back, and to contemplate the
condition of the northern nations upon their first settlement
in those countries which they occupied. It is necessary to,
mark the great steps by which they advanced from barba-
rifm to refinement, and to point out those general principles
and events which, by their uniform as well as extensive ope-
ration, conducted all of them to that degree of improvement
in policy and in manners which they had attained at-the pe-
riod when Charles V. began his reign.

When nations subjec to despotic government make con-

quests, these serve only to extend the dominion and the power
of their master. Bu armies composed of freemen conquer
for themselves, not for their leaders. The people who over-
turned the Roman empire, and fettled in its various pro-
vinces, were of the latter class. Not only the different na-
tions that issued from the north of Europe, which has al-
ways been confidered as the Rate of liberty, but the Huns.
and Alans who inhabited part of those countries, which have
been marked out as the peculiar region of servitude*, enjoy.
ed freedom and independence in such a high degree as seems
to be scarcely compatible with a state of social union, or

As the conquerors of Europe had their acquisitions to

scarcely diminilhed their personal independence, had been
sufficient for their security while they remained in their ori-

rights in order to attain public safety. Every freeman, up-
on receiving a portion of the lands which were divided, bound
himself to appear in arms against the enemies of the commu-
nity. This military service was the condition upon which
he received and held his lands; and as they were exempted
from every other burden, that tenure, among a warlike people
was deemed both easy and honourable. The king or gene-
ral who led them to conquest, continuing till to be the bead
of the colony, had, of course, the largest portion allotted to
him. Having thus acquired the means of rewarding past
services, as well as of gaining new adherents, he parcelled
out his lands with this view, binding those on whom they were
bestowed to resort to his fiandard with a number of men in
proportion to the extent of the territory which they received,
and to bear arms in his defence. His chief officers imitated
the example of the sovereign, and, in distributing portions of
their lands among their dependents, annexed the fame condi-
tion to the grant. Thus a feudal kingdom resembled a mili-
tary establishment, rather than a civil institution. The vic-
torious army, cantoned out in the country which it had feiz-
ed, continued ranged under its proper officers, and fubordi-
nate to military-command. The names of a soldier and of
a freeman were synonymous-. Every proprietor of land,-girt
with a sword, was ready to march at the summons of his
superior, and to take the field against the common ene-
my.

But though the feudal policy seems to be so' dmirably

were dissolved. A kingdom, considerable in name and in

Such was the state of Europe with refpec to the interior

administration of government from the seventh to the ele.
venth century. All the external operations of its various
flates, during this period, were, of course, extremely feeble.
A kingdom dismembered, and torn with dissension, without
any common interest to route, or any common head to con-
duct its force, was incapable of acting with vigour. AlmoR
all the wars in Europe, during the-ages which I have men-
tioned,'were trifling, indecisive, and productive of no confi-

on some petty enterprize, to which he was prompted by his

own ambition or revenge. The Rate itself, destitute of uni-

rope.

To these pernicious effecs of the feudal anarchy may be

added its fatal influence on the chara&er and improvement

regular government, together with the expectation of per.

uring four centuries, produced few authors who merit to

hat long period can boast.

As the inhabitants of Europe, during there centuries,

were strangers to the arts which embelliih a polished age,
they were destitute of the virtues which abound among peo-

V. The various expedients which were employed in order

With this fuperRitious opinion, the martial spirit of Eu-

rope, during the middle ages, concurred in eftablifhling the
mode of trial by judicial combat. To beready to maintain

pride of high-fpirited nobles. The form of trial by com-

T'uus the form of trial by combat, like other abuse,

spread gradually, and extended to all persons, and almost to

The folemnities of a judicial combat were such as were na-

they were ambitious to attaint.

By this barbarous custom, the natural course of proceed-

ing, both in civil and criminal questions, was entirely per-
verted. Force usurped the place of equity in courts of
judicature, and justice was banished from her proper mansion.
Discernment, learning, integrity, were qualities less necef-
fary to a judge, than bodily strength and dexterity in the
ule of arms. Daring courage, and superior vigour or ad-
dress, were of more moment towards securing the favour-
able issue of a suit, than the-equity of a cause, or the clear-
nefa of the evidence. Men, of course, applied themselves to
cultivate the talents which they found to be of great utili-
ty. As strength of body and address in arms were no less
requisite in those lifts which they were obliged to enter in de-
fence of their private rights, than in the field of battle,
where they met the enemies of their country, it becamre the
great object of their education, as well as the chief employ.
ment of life, to acquire these martial accomplilhments. The
adminiltration of justice, instead of accufloming men to lift-

Among the rude people who conquered the various pro.

Thus, by the natural operation of circumstances peculiar

X. The progress of commerce had considerable influence

The ordinary revenues of every prince were so extremely

As there circumstances rendered the operations of particu-

The next event of confcquence in the fifteenth century,

own intentions, he laboured to prevail with him to march

The power which Charles possessed was so great, that he

But so different were the efforts of the states of Europe

in the fifteenth century, from those which we shall behold
in the course of this history, that the army with which
Charles undtr-ook this great enterprize, did not exceed
twenty thousand men. The train of artillery, however, the
ammunition, and warlike stores of every kind provided for

its use, were so considerable, as to bear some resemblance to

When the French entered Italy, they met with nothing

able to refill them. The Italian powers having remained,

of service, were terrified at the aspet of real war, and shrunk
at its approach. The impetuosity of the French valour ap-
peared to them irrefiftable. Florence, Pisa, and Rome o-
pened their gates as the French army advanced. The pro.
speCt of this dreadful invasion itruck one king of Naples

polition, as if he had been on a progrets through his owna-

and intimidated or gave law to every power in Italy.

Such was the conclusion of an expedition, that mufl be
confidered as the firit great exertion of those new powers

The sudden and decisive effe& of thi- confederacy, seems

them. They had extended, on this occasion, to the affairs

of Europe, the maxims of that political science which had
hitherto been applied only to regulate the operations of the
petty states in their own country. They had discovered the
method of preventing any monarch from rising to such a
degree of power, as was inconsistent with the general liber-

great secret in modern policy. the preservation of a proper
diltribution of power among all the members of the fyllem
into which the states of Europe are formed. During all the

this era we can trace the progress of that intercourse between
nations, which has linked the powers of Europe so closely
together; and can discern the operations of that provident
policy, which, during peace, guards against remote and con.
tingent dangers; and, in war, has prevented rapid and de-
fru&ive conquests.

This was not the only effet of the operations which the

This innovation in the military system was quickly follow.

ed by another, which the custom of employing Swiss in the
Italian wars was the occasion of introducing. The arms and
discipline of the Swiss were different from those of other Eu-
ropean nations. During their long and violent struggles in

armies, like those of other considerable princes, confited
chiefly of heavy-armed cavalry, the Swiss found that their

their power to bring into the field any body ofhorfe capable

their confidence in infantry; and in order to render it capa-
ble of withflanding the Ihock of cavalry, they gave the fol-
diers brea!t-plates and helmets as defensive armour; together
with long spears, halberts, and heavy swords, as weapons of
offence. They formed them into large battalions ranged in
deep and close array, so that they could present on every fide
a formidable front to the enemy*. The men at arms could
make no impression on the solid strength of such a body. It
repulsed the Austrians in all their attempts to conquer Swif-

The Spaniards, whose situation made it difficult to em-

The Italian flates gradually diminiihed the number of their

As their efforts in Italy led the people of Europe to there

improvements in the art of war, they gave them likewise the
firit idea of the expence with which it is accompanied when

elI military operations. But when Italy became the theatre

fall, that it was impofible at first to eftablilh funds propor-

When Charles VII. invaded Naples, the fums requisite for

government, that before he reached the frontiers of Italy,

es unusual burdens; the only expedient that remained was,

From this period, taxes went on increasing; and during the

would have appeared enormous at the close of the fifteenth

exorbitant exactions of modern times.

The last tranfa&ion, previous to the reign of Charles V.

that merits attention on account of its influence upon the

opulent fate in Europe.

The power of the Venetians was the object of terror to

Julius, elated with beholding the effects of a league which

Nothing was wanting to render this empire absolute, and;

but the spirit which conduced them was always the fame.
While the measures of other governments fliu&uated, and
the objects at which they aimed varied, the church kept one
end in view; and to this unrelaxing constancy of pursuit, it
was indebted for its success in the boldest attempts ever
inade by human ambition.

But in their civil administration, the popes followed no

such uniform or consistent plan. There, as in other govern-
ments, the characer, the palfions, and the interest of the
person who had the supreme direcion of affairs, occasioned
a variation both in objects and measures. As few prelates
reached the summit of ecclesiastical dignity until they
were far advanced in life, a change of matiers were more
frequent in the papal dominions than in other states, and the
political system was, of course, less liable and permanent.
Every pope was eager to make the most of the short period,
during which he had the profpec of enjoying power, in or-
der to aggrandize his own family, and to attain his private
ends; and it was often the first buflnefs of his successor to
undo all that he had done, and to overturn what he had e-
stablished.

As ecclesiastics were trained to pacific arts, and early in-

itiated in the mytleries of that policy by which the court of
Rome extended or supported its spiritual dominion, the
popes in the conduc of their temporal affairs were apt
to follow the fame maxims, and in all their measures were
more ready to employ the refinements of intrigue, than the
force of arms. It was in the papal court that address and
rubtlety in negotiation became a science; and during the

f.xteenth century, Rome was confidered as the school in
which it might be beit acquired.

As the decorum of their ecclefiar.ical character prevented

the popes from placing tlhcmfelves at the head of their armies,
or fiom taking the command in person of the military force
in their dominions, they were afraid to arm their subje&s;
and in all their operations, whether offensive or defensive,
they trusted entirely to mercenary troops.

As their power and dominions could not descend to their

poicerity, the popes were less felicitous than other prince
to form or to encourage schemes of public utility and im.
provement. Their tenure was only for a short life; pre-
sent advantage was what they chiefly studied; to squeeze
and to amass, rather than to meliorate, was their objea.
They erected perhaps, some work of ostentation, to remain
as a monument of their pontificate; they found it necessary
at some times, to establish uftful inflitutions, in order to
soothe and silence the turbulent populace of Rome; but
plans of general benefit to their subjects, framed with a view
to futurity, were rarely objecs of attention in the papal po-
licy. The patrimony of St. Peter was worse governed than
any part of Europe; and though a generous pontiffmight
suspend for a little, or countera& the effccs of those vices
which are peculiar to the administration of ecclesiastics; the
disease not only remained without remedy, but has gone os
increasing from age to age; and the decline of the state has
kept pace with its progrcfs.

One circumnlance, farther, concerning the papal govern.

meat, is so singular, as to merit attention. As the spiritual
fipremacy and temporal power were united in one person,
and uniformly aided each other in their operations, they be-
came so blended together, that it was difficult to separate
them, even in imagination. The potentates, who found it
necefary to oppose the measures which the popes purfuea
as temporal princes, could not easily divest themselves of the
reverence which they imagined to be due to them as heads

of the church, and vicars of Jesus Christ. It was with re-
lu:jan¢e that they could be brought to a rupture with the
head of the church; they were unwilhlng to pulh their opera-
tions against himto extremity; they listened eagerly to the firit
overtures of accommodation, and were anxious to procure
it almoit upon any terms. Their consciousness of this en-
couraged the enterpriling pontiffs who filled the papal throne
about the beginning of the sixteenth century, to engage in
schemes seemingly the most extravagant. They trusted,
that if their temporal power was not sufficient to carry them
through with success, the refpe& paid to their spiritual dig-
Rity would enable them to extricate themselves with facility
and with honour*. But when popes came to take part
more frequently in the contests among princes, and to en-
gage as principals or auxiliaries in every war kindled in
Europe, this veneration for their sacred character began to
abate; and striking instances will occur in the following
hiflory of its being almost totally extint.

The rife of that commonwealth, during the inroads of the

If we view the Venetian government as calculated for the

The spirit of government, in a commonwealth of this

species, was, of course, timid and jealous. The Venetian
nobles distrusted their own fubjeds, and were afraid of allow.
ing them the use of arms. They encouraged among them the
arts of industry and commerce; they employed them in ma.
nufatures and in navigation, but never admitted them into
the troops which the state kept in its pay. The military
force of the republic consisted entirely of foreign mercena-
ries. The command of these was never trusted to noble
Venetians, left they Ihould acquire such influence over the
army, as might endanger the public liberty; or become ac-
cultomed to the exercise of such power, as would make them
unwilling to return to the condition of private citizens. A
soldier of fortune was placed at the head of the armies of the
commonwealth; and to obtain that honour, was the great
obje& of the Italian Condottieri, or leaders of bands, who,
in the fifteenth and sixteenth centuries, made a trade of war,
and raised and hired out soldiers to different flates. But the
fame fufpicics policy, which induced the Venetians to emr
ploy these adventurers, prevented their placing entire confl
dence in them. Two noblemen, appointed by the senate,
accompanied their army, when it took the field, with the ap

pellation of Prove&litori, and, like the field-deputies of the
Dutch republic in latter times, observed all the motions of
the general, and checked and controuled him in all his ope-
rations.

A commonwealth with such civil and military institutions,

was not formed to make conquests. While its fubjets were
disarmed, and its nobles excluded from military command, it
carried on its warlike enterprizes with great disadvantage.
This ought to have taught the Venetians to reft satisfied
with making felf-prefervation, and the enjoyment of domes-
tic security, the obje&s of their policy. But republics are
apt to be seduced by the spirit of ambition, as well as kings.
When the Venetians so far forgot the interior defecs in
their government as to aim at extensive conquests, the fatal
blow, which they received in the war excited by the league
of Cambray, convinced them of the imprudence and danger
of making violent efforts, in opposition to the genius and
tendency of their constitution.

It is not, however, by its military, but by its naval and

commercial power, that the importance of the Venetian com-
monwealth muff be estimated. The latter conifituted the
real force and nerves of the state. The jealousy of govern-
ment did not extend to this department. Nothing was ap-
prehended from this quarter, that could prove formidable to
liberty. The senate encouraged the nobles to trade, and to
serve on board the fleet. They became merchants and ad-
mirals. They increased the wealth of their country by their
industry. They added to its dominions, by the valour with
which they conducted its naval armaments.

Commerce was an inexhaustible source of opulence to the
Vecntians. All the nations in Europe depended upon them,
not only for the commodities of the Eafl, but for various
manufautures fabricated by them alone, or finiffed with a
dexterity and elegance unknown in other countries. From
this extensive commerce, the state derived such immense fiip-
plies, as concealed those vices in its couftitution wLich I

In Aragon, the form of government was monarchical, but

the genius and maxims of it were purely republican. The
kings who were long elective, retained only the ihadow -of
power; the real exercise of it was in the Cortes or parlia-
ment of the kingdom. This supreme assembly was com-
porfd of four different arms or members. The nobility of
the first rank. The equestrian order, or nobility of the fe-
cond class. The reprcfentatives of the cities and towns
whose right to a place in the Cortes, if we may give creditto
the historians of Aragon, was coevai with the constitution.
The ecclesiastical order, composed of the dignitaries of the
church, together with the representatives of the inferior
clergy*. No law could pass in this assembly without the
aftent of every single member who had a right to votet.
Without the permission of the Cortes, no tax could be im-
posed; no war could be declared; no peace could be con-
cluded; no money could be coined; nor could any altera-
tion be made in the current fpeciee:. The power of review-
ing the proceedings of all inferior courts, the privilege of in-
fpefting every department of adminifiration, and the right of
redressing all grievances, belonged to the Cortes. Nor did
those who conceived themselves to be aggrieved, address the
Cortes in the humble tone of fuppliaits, and petition for
redress; they demanded it as the birthright of freemen, and
required the guardians of their liberty to decide with refpcct
tothe points which theylaid before tlem§. This fove-
reign court was held during several centuries, every year;
but, in consequence of a regulation introduced about the be-
ginning of the fourteenth century, it was convoked from
that period only once in two years. After it was assembled,
the king had no right to prorogue or dissolve it without its
own content; and the feflion continued forty days ¶.

The conltitutions of the subordinate monarchies, which

depended on the crowns of Castile and Aragon, nearly refem-

In all of them, the dignity and independence of the nobles

considerable.

An attentive observation of the singular situation of

Spain, as well as the various events which occurred there,
from the invasion of the Moors to the union of its king-
doms under Ferdinand and Isabella, will discover the causes
to which all the peculiarities in its political constitution I
have pointed out, ought to be ascribed.

As the provinces of Spain were wrested from the Maho-

metans gradually and with difficulty, the nobles who follow-
ed the standard of any eminent leader in these wars, conquer-
ed not for him alone, but for themselves. They claimed a
ihare in the lands which their valour had won from the ene-
ny, and their prosperity and power increased, in proportion
as the territory of the prince extended.

During their perpetual wars with the Moors, the mo.

narchs of the several kingdoms in Spain depended so much
on their nobles, that it became necessary to conciliate their
good-will by successive grants of new honours and privi.
leges. By the time that any prince could establish his do.
minion in a conquered province, the greater part of the
territory was parcelled out by him among his barons, with
such jurisdiction and immunities as raised them almost to
sovereign power.

At the fame time, the kingdoms ere&ed in so many dif.

ferent corners of Spain, were of inconsiderable extent.
The petty monarch was but little elevated above his noble&
They, feeling themselves to be almost his equals, a&ed as
such; and could not look up to the kings of such limited
domains with the fame reverence that the sovereigns of the
great monarchies in Europe were viewed by their subje&s
ELL].

While these circumstances concurred in exalting the no-

bility, and in depressing the royal authority, there were o-
ther causes which raised the cities in Spain to consideration
and power.

As the open country, during the wars with the Moors,

was perpetually exposed to the incursions of the enemy, with
whom no peace or truce was so permanent as to prove any
lalting security, felf-prefervation obliged persons of all ranks
to fix their residence in places of strength. The castles of
the barons, which, in other countries, afforded a commodi-
ous retreat from the depredations of banditti, or from the
transient violence of any interior commotion, were unable to
resist an enemy whose operations were coaduted with regu-
lar and preserving vigour. Cities, in which great numbers
united for their mutual defence, were the only places in
which people could reside with any profpea of safety. To
this was owing the rapid growth of those cities in Spain of
which the Christians recovered pofflffion. All who fled

[LL] Note XXXIV.

from the Moorish yoke resorted to them, as to an asylum;
and in them. the greater part of those who took the field
against the Mahometans eltablifhed their families.

Several of those cities, during a longer or shorter course

of Years, were the capitals of little states, and enjoyed all
the advantages which accelerate the increase of inhabitants
in every place that is the feat of government.

From those concurring causes, the number of cities in

Spain, at the beginning of the fifteenth century, had become
considerable, and they were peopled far beyond 'the propor-
tion which was common in other parts of Europe, except in
Italy and the Low-Countries. The Moors had introduced
manufactures into those cities, while under their dominion.
The Christians, who, by intermixture with them, had learn-
ed their arts, continued to cultivate these. Trade in several
of the Spanish towns appears to have been carried on with
vigour; and the spirit of commerce continued to preserve
the number of their inhabitants, as the sense of danger had
firit induced them to crowd together.

As the Spanish cities were populous, many of the inhabi-

tants were of a rank superior to those who resided in towns
in other countries of Europe. That cause, which contri-
buted chiefly to their population, affeEted equally persons of
every condition, who flocked thitherpromifcuoufly, in order to
find shelter thereor in hopes of making aftand againftthe ene-

ble rank in the kingdom, as refleted lustre on their conllitu-
ents, and on the stations wherein they were placed.

As it was impossible to carry on a continual war against

the Moors, without some other military force than that
wiich the barons were obliged to bring into the field, in
-cofilquence of the feudal tenures, it became necessary to

have some troops, particularly a body of light cavalry, ia
conilant pay. It was one of the privileges of the nobles,
that their lands were exempt from the burden of taxes
The charge of supporting the troops requilite for the pub.
lic safety, fell wholly upon the cities; and their kings, be.
ing obliged frequently to apply to them for aid, found it ne.
cclTary to gain their favour by concessions, which not only
extend&d their immunities, but added to their wealth and

When the influence of all these circumstances, peculiar to

Spain, is added to the general and common causes, which
contrbuted to aggrandize cities in other countries of Eu.
rope, this wil fully account for the extensive privileges
which they acquired, as well as for the extraordinary con.
fideration to which they attained, in all the Spanifi kingdoms

By there exorbitant privileges of the nobility, and this

unusual power of the cities in Spain, the royal prerogative

the emperor's immediate jurifdi&ion, he restored some de-
gree of vigour to the Imperial authority.

and improvements, the political constitution of the German
empire, at the commencement of the period of which I pro-

to resemble perfe&tly any form of government known ct.her

Under this afpe&, the conflitution of the empire appears

a regular confederacy, similar to the Achaean league in an.
cient Greece, or to that of the United Provinces and of the
Swiss Cantons in modern times. But if viewed in another

princes and flates, joined in this association, were originally

iection was entirely at an end, and the influence of the feu-

former confidered the emperor only as the head of a confe-
deracy, the members of which, by their voluntary choice,
have raised him to that dignity; the latter seemed to imply,
that he is really invested with sovereign power. By this
circumitance, such principles of hoffility. and' discord were
interwoven into the frame of the Germanic body,. as asset-
ed each of its members, rendering their interior union in-
complete, and their external efforts feeble and irregular..
The pernicious influence of this defe& inherent in the con-
litution of the empire is so considerable, that, without at-
tending to it, we cannot fully comprehend many tranfac.
tions in the reign of Charles V. or form just ideas concern-
hig the genius of the German government.

The emperors of Germany, at the beginning of the fix-
teenth century, were distinguished by the most pompous
titles, and by such ensigns of dignity, as intimated their au-
thority to be superior to that of all other monarchs. The
greatctr princes of the empire attended, and served them, on;
3ine occasions as the officers of their household. They ex-
rTifed prerogatives which no other sovereign ever claimed.
They. retained pretensions to all the extensive powers which
their predecessors had enjoyed in any former age. But, at
the fame time, instead of pofeffing that ample domain which
had belonged to the ancient emperors of Germany, and;
which stretched from Basil to Cologne, along both banks of
the Rhine*, they were stripped of'all territorial property,
and had not a single city, a single cafile, a single foot of land,-
that belonged' to them, as heads of the empire. As their
domain was alienated, their ftated&revenues were reduced-
almost to nothing; and the extraordinary aids,.which. on a,
ft- occasions they obtained, were granted sparingly and.
paid with relu&tance. The princes and states of the empire,,

though they seemed to recognize the Imperial authority,
.were fubjeds only in name, each of them possessing a com.

From this ill-compaaed frame of government, effe&s that

were unavoidable resulted. The emperors, dazzled with
the splendour of their titles, and the external signs of vast
:authority, were apt to imagine themselves to be the real so.
-ereigns of Germany, and were led to aim continually at re-

joyed. The princes and states, aware of the nature as well
as extent of these pretensions, were perpetually on their
guard, in order to watch all the motions of the Imperial
court, and to circumscribe its power within limits till more
narrow. The emperors, in support of their claims, appeal-
ed to ancient forms and institutions, which the states held
to be obsolete. The flates founded their rights on recent
practice and modern privileges, which the emperors confider.
ed as usurpations.

This jealousy of the Imperial authority, together with

the opposition betw-een it and the rights of the iates, in.
created considerably from the time that the emperors were
elected, not by the colle&tive body of German nobles, but
by a few princes of chief dignity. During a long period,
all the members of the Germanic body had a right to as-
iemble, and to make choice of the person whom they ap-
pointed to be their head. But amidi the violence and anar-
chy wh'ch prevailed for several centuries in the empire,
fcv-nk prines who pofffled the most extensive territories, and
who had obtained a hereditary title to the great offices of
the -late, acquired the exclusive privilege of nominating the
emperor. This right was confirmed to them by the Gold-
en Bull: the mod- of exercising it was ascertained, and they
were dignified \ith the appellation of Eleaors. The nobi-

lity and free cities being thus stripped of a privilege which
they had once enjoyed, were less conneced with a prince,
towards whose elevation they had not contributed by their
suffrages, and came to be more apprehensive of his authori-
ty. The electors, by their extensive power, and the diftin-
guifhing privileges which they pofleffed, became formida-
ble to the emperors, with whom they were placed almost on
a level in several a&s of jurisdiCtion. Thus the introduntion
of the eledoral college into the empire, and the authority
which it acquired, instead of diminishing, contributed to
firengthen, the principles of hotility and discord in the
Germanic constitution;

These were further augmented by the various and repug-

nant forms of civil policy in the several states which compos-
ed the Germanic body. It is no easy matter to render the
union of independent Rtates perfect and entire, even when
the genius and forms of their refpe&ive governments hap-
pen to be altogether similar. But in the Germanic empire,
which was a confederacy of princes, of ecclesiastics, and of
free cities, it was impossible that they could incorporate
thoroughly. The free cities were small republics, in which
the maxims and spirit peculiar to that species of government
prevailed. The princes and nobles, to whom supreme jurif-
diEtion belonged, possessed a fort of monarchical power with.
in their own territories, add the forms of their interior ad-
ministration nearly resembled those of the great feudal king-
doms. The interests, the ideas, the obje¢ts of iates so dif-
ferently confituted, cannot be the fame. Nor could their
common deliberations be carried on with the fame spirit,
while the love of liberty, and attention to commerce, were
the reigning principles in the cities; while the desire of
lpower, and ardour for military glory,were the governing pas-
sions of the princes and nobility.

The secular and ecclesiastical members of the empire were

as little fitted for union as the free cities and the nobility.
Considerable territories had been granted to several of the

bishoprics and abbeys, and some of the highest offices in the
empire having been annexed to them inalienably, were held
by the ecclfiaftics raised to these dignities. The younger
sons of noblemen of the second order, who hal devoted
themselves to the church, were commonly promoted to these
stations of eminence and powers and it was no small mor-
tification to the princes and great nobility, to fee persons
raised from an inferior rank to the fame level with themselves,
or even exalted to superior dignity. The education of these
churchmen, the genius of their profeflion, and their connec-
tion with the court of Rome, rendered their charater as
well as their interest different from those of the other mem-
bers of the Germanic body, with whom they werw called to
ad in concert. Thus another source ofjealoufy and vari-
ance was opened, which ought not to be overlooked when
we are searching into the nature of the German confiitu.
tion.

To all these causes of dissension may be added one more,

arising from the unequal distribution of power and wealth
among the states of the empire. The eleEtors, and other
nobles of the highest rank, not only poffelfed sovereign ju-
rifdicion, but governed such extensive, populous, and rich
countries, as rendered them great princes. Many of the o-
ther members, though they enjoyed all the rights of fove-
reignty, ruled over such petty domains, that their real pow-
er bore no proportion to this high prerogative. A well
compa&ed and vigorous confederacy could not be formed
of such dissimilar itates. The weaker were jealous, timid,
and unable either to assert or to defend their just privileges.
The more powerful were apt to assume and to become op.
preffive. The ele&ors, and emperors, by turns, endeavoured
to extend their own authority, by encroaching on those fee-
ble members of the Germanic body, who sometimes defend-
ed their rights with much spirit, but more frequently, being
overawed or corrupted, they tamely surrendered their pri-

vileges, or meanly favoured the designs formed against them
[TT].

After contemplating all these principles of disunion and

opposition in the constitution of the German empire, it will
be easy to account for the want of concord and uniformity,
conspicuous in its councils and proceedings. That low, di-
latory, distrustful, and irresolute spirit, which chara&erizes
all its deliberations, will appear natural in a body, the junc-
tion of whose members was so incomplete, the different parts
of which were held together by such feeble ties, and set at
variance by such powerful motives. But the empire of Ger-
many, nevertheless, comprehended countries of such great
extent, and was inhabited by such a martial and hardy race
of men, that when the abilities of an emperor, or zeal for
any common cause, could rouse this unwieldy body to put
forth its strength, it a&ed with almost irresistible force. In
the following history we lhall find, that as the measures
on which Charles V. was most intent, were often thwarted
or rendered abortive by the spirit ofjealoufy and division
peculiar to the Germanic constitution; so it was by the in-
fluence which he acquired over the princes of the empire,
and by engaging them to co-operate with him, that he was
enabled to make some of the greatest efforts which diftin.
guilh his reign.

The Turkish history isofo blended, during the reign of

Charles V. with that of the great nations in Europe, and
the Ottoman Porte interposed so often, and with such deci-
five influence, in the wars and negotiations of the Christian
princes, that some previous account of the state of govern.
ment in that great empire, is no less necessary for the infor.
mation of my readers, than those views of the constitution
of other kingdoms which I have already exhibited to them.

It has been the fate of the southern and more fertile parts

of Asia, at different periods, to be conquered by that warlike
and hardy race of men, who inhabit the vati country known

[TTJ Note XLII.

to the ancients by the name of Scythia, and among the
moderns by that of Tartary. One tribe of these people,
called Turks or Turcomans, extended its conquests, under
various leaders, and during several centuries, from the Caf.
pian Sea to the straits of the Dardanelles. Towards the
middle of the fifteenth century, these formidable conquerors
took Constantinople by torm, and eftablilhed the feat of
their government in that imperial city. Greece, Moldavia,
Walachia, and the other provinces of the ancient kingdoms
of Thrace and Macedonia, together with part of Hungary,
were fubjeted to their power.

But though the feat of the Turkish government was fix.

ed in Europe, and the sultans obtained possession of such ex.
tensive dominions in that quarter of the globe, the genius
of their policy continued to be purely Asiatic; and may be
properly termed a despotism, in contradiftinaion to those
monarchical and republican forms of government which we
have been hitherto contemplating. The supreme power
was vested in sultans of the Ottoman race, that blood being
deemed so sacred, that no other was thought worthy of the
throne. From this elevation, these sovereigns could look
down and behold all their subje&s reduced to the fame level
before them. The maxims of Turkish policy do not au-
thorife any of those inflitutions, which in other countries,
limit the exercise, or moderate the rigour of monarchical
power; they admit neither of any great court with confi-
tutional and permanent jurifdi&tion to interpose, both in en-
afting laws, and in superintending the execution of them;
nor of a body of hereditary nobies, whose sense of their own
pre-eminence, whose consciousness of what is due to their
rank and character, whose jealousy of their privileges cir-
cumfcribe the authority of the prince, and serve not only
as a barrier againf the excesses of his caprice, but stand as
an intermediate order between him and the people. Under
the Turkish government, the political condition of every
subjec is equal. To be employed in the fcrvice of the ful-

tan, is the only circumstance that confers diftinlion. Even
this diftinftion is rather official than personal, and so closely
annexed to the fation in which any individual serves, that it
is scarcely communicated to the persons of those who are
placed in them. The ighellt dignity in the empire does
not give any rank or pre-eminence to the family of him who
enjoys it. As every man, before he is raised to any station
of authority, must go through the preparatory discipline of a
long and servile obedience*, the moment he is deprived of
power, he and his posterity return to the fame condition
with other fubjeas, and fink back into obscurity. It is the
distinguishing and odious charaferiftic of Eastern despotism
that it annihilates all other ranks of-men, in order to exalt
the monarch; that it leaves nothing to the former, while it
gives every thing to the latter; that it endeavours to fix in the
minds of those who are fibjec to it, the idea of no relation
between men, but that of a mailer and of a slave, the for-
mer defined to command and to punish, the latter formed
to tremble and to obey [UU].

But as there are circurftances which frequently obflrut

or defeat the salutary effeas of the befl regulated govern-
ments, there are others which contribute to mitigate the
evils of the most defective forms of policy. There can, in-
deed, be no contitutional restraints upon the will of a
prince in a despotic government; but there may be such
as are accidental. Absolute as the Turkish sultans are,
they feel themselves circumscribed both by religion, the
principle on which their authority is foundedt, and by the
army, the instrument which they mutl employ in order to
maintain it. Wherever religion interposes, the will of the
sovereign muff submit to its decrees. When the Koran
hath prescribed any religious rite, hath enjoined any moral
duty, or hath confirmed by its fantion any political maxim,

nencet. The Janizaries soon became the chief strength
and pride of the Ottoman armies; and, by their number as

nod, at other times, life and death depend.

From Mahomet II. who took Constantinople, to Solyman
the Magnificent, who began his reign a few months after
'Charles V. was placed on the Imperial throne of Germany,
a succession of illustrious princes ruled over the Turkibh em-
pire. By their great abilities, they kept their fubjets of
every order, military as well as civil, submissive to govern-
ment; and had the absolute command of whatever force their
vast empire was able to exert. Solyman, in particular, who
is known to the Christians chiefly as a conqueror, but is ce-
lebrated in the Turkifi annals, as the great lawgiver who
established order and police in their empire, governed, dur-
ing his long reign, with no less authority than wisdom. He
divided his dominions into several diftrias;. -he appointed the.
number offoldiers which each fliould furnish; he appropri-
ated a certain proportion of the land in every province f6r
their maintenance; hie regulated, with a minute accuracy,
every thing relative to their difcipline,'-heir arms, and the'-
nature of their service. He put the'finances of the empire
into an orderlytrain of administration; and, though the tax4
es in the THrkifh dominions, as well as in the other de4potic
monarchies of the EAst, are far fref bbeing co1fiderable, .'

Nor was it only under such sultans as Solyman, whose ta.

lents were no less adapted to preserve internal order than to
conduc the operations of war, that the Turkish empire en.
gaged with advantage in its contests with the Chrifian ifates.
The long succession of able princes, which I have mentioned,
had given such vigour and firmness to the Ottoman govern.
nent, that it seems to have attained, during the sixteenth cen.
tury, the highefl degree of perfe&ion of which its conftitu.
t'on was capable. Whereas the great monarchies in Christ-
endom were till far from that state, which could enable them
to at with a full exertion of their force. Besides this, the
Turki]a troops in that age pofli'eed every advantage which
arises from superiority in military discipline. At the time
'when Solyman began his reign, the Janizaries had been em.
bodied near a century and a half; and, during that long pe-
riod, the severity of their military discipline had in no de-
gree relaxed. The other soldiers, drawn from the provin.
ces of the empire, had been kept almost continually under
arms, in the -various wars which the sultans had carried on,
with hardly any interval of peace. Against troops thus train.
ed and accustomed to service, the forces of the Christian
powers took the field with great disadvantage. The most
intelligent as well as impartial authors of the sixteenth cen.
tury acknowledge and lament the superior attainments of the
Turks in the military art [YY]. The success which almod
uniformly attended their arms, in all their wars, demongtrates
the juftneis of this ohfervation. The Christian armies did
not acquire that superiority over the Turks, which they now
possess, until the long establishment of standing forces had
improved military discipline among the former; and until
various canses and events, which it is not my province to
explain, had corrupted or abolished their ancient warlike in-
Ilitutiona among the latter.

But the late in which Italy appears to have been, during fevte

ral ages after the barbarous nations fettled in it, is the most de.
cifive proof of the cruelty as well as extent of their devaflations.
MWhenever any country is thinly inhabited, trees and firubs
spring up in the uncultivated fields, and spreading by degrees,
form large forests; by the overflowing of rivers, and the flagnat.
ing of waters, other parts of it are converted into lakes-and marsh-
es. Ancient Italy, which the Romans rendered the feat of ele-
gance and luxury, was cultivated to the highest pitch. But so es-
feclually did the devastations of the barbarians destroy all the es-
se&c of Roman industry and cultivation, that in the eighth century
a confidtrable part of Italy appears to have been covered with so-
rcft and m.arshes of great extent. Muratori enters into a mi-
nute detail concerning the situation and limits of several of there;
and proves by the most authentic evidence, that great tracds of
territory, in all the different provinces of Italy, were either over-
run with wood, or laid under water. Nor did there occupy parts
of the country natura'ly barren or of little value, but were spread
over diftricls which ancient writers represent as extremely fertile,
and which at prelent are highly cultivated. Muratori Antiqui-
tates Italica medii evi, dissert. xxi. v. ii. p. 149, I53, &c. A
strong proof of this occurs in a description of the city of Modena,
by an author of the tenth century. Murat. Script. Rerum Italic.
vol. ii. pars ii. p. 691. The state of desolation in other countries
of Europe teems to have been the fame. In many of the most
eaTlv charters now extant, the lands granted to monasteries, or to
private persons, are distinguished into such as are cultivated or
inhabited, and such as were ermui, desolate. In many instances,
lands are granted to persons because they had taken them from
the desert, 4 ercmo, and had cultivated and planted them with
inhabitants. This appears from a charter of Charlemagne,
publifed by Eckhart de Rebus Francie Orientalis, vol. ii. p. 864,
and from many charters of his successors quoted by Du Cange,
voc. eremus.-Wherever a right of property in land can be thus
acquired, it is evident that the country must be extremely desolate
and thinly peopled. The first settlers in America obtained pof
feCon of land by such a title. Whoever was able to clear and t(
cultivate a field, was recognized as the proprietor. His induftr
merited flch a recompence. The grants in the charters which
have mentioned flow from a similar principle, and there mull hav
Leen some referblance in the fate of the countries.

Muratori adds, that during the eighth and ninth centuries, Ita

ly was greatly infefled with wolves and other wild bealts; ano
ther mark ot its being destitute of inhabitants. Murat. Antiq
vol. ii. p. 163. Thus Italy, the pride of the ancient world for it
fertility and cultivation, was reduced to the Rate of a countr
newly peopled and lately rendered habitable.

I am sensible, not only that some of these decrliptions of the de

variations, which I have quoted, may be exaggerated, but that th
barbarous tribes, in making their fetlements, did not proceed ii

of the human species. occasioned by the hostile invasions of the

NOTE VI. SeC. I. p. s8. [F].

I have observed, Note II that our only certain information
concerning the ancient state of tie barbarous nations must be de-
rived from the Greek and Roman writers. Happily an account
of the inflitutions and customs of one people, to which those of all
the reft seem to have been in a great measure similar, has been
transmitted to us by two authors, the most capable, perhaps that
ever wrote, of observing them with profound discernment, and of
delcribing them with propriety and force. The reader mull per-
ceive that Cuefar and Tacitus are the authors whom I have in
view. The former gives a short account of the ancient Germans
in a few chapters of the sixth book of his Commentaries; the lat-
ter wrote a treatise expressly on that subjed. Thele are the most
precious and infirudtive monuments of antiquity to the present
inhabitants of Europe. From them we learn,

i. That the state of society among the ancient Germans was

of the rudest and most simple form. They lubfifled entirely by
hunting or by pasturage. Caf. lib. vi. c. 21. They negleced
agriculture, and lived chiefly on milk, cheese, and flefih Ibid. c.
22. Tacitus agrees with him in moit of thele points. De morib.
Germ. c. 14, 1 , 23. The Goths were equally negligent of agri-
culture. Prifc. Rhet. ap. Byz. Script. v. i. p. 3. B. Society
was in the fame itate among the Huns, who dildained to cultivate
the earth, or to touch a plough. Amm. Marcel. lib. xxxi. p. 475.
The fame manners took place among the Alans; ibid. p. 477.
While lbciety remains in this finple itate, men by uniting toge-
ther scarcely relinquish any portion of their natural independence.
Accordingly we are informed, z. That the authority of civil
government was extremely limited among the Germans. Dur-
ing times of peace they had no common or fixed magistrate, but
the chief men of every diftria difpenled justice and accommodated
diffcrences, Cef. ibid. c. 23. Their kings had not abbolute or
unbounded power; their authority confifed rather in the privi-
lege of advising, than in the power of commanding. Matters of
fniall consequence were determined by the chief men; affairs of
importance by the whole community. Tacit. c. 7. 11. The
luns, in like manner, deliberated in common concerning every
business of moment to the society; and were not subject to the
rigour of regal authority. Amm. Marcel. lib. xxxi. p. 474.
S. Every individual among the ancient Gcrmlans was left at li-
berty to choose whither he would take part in any military ca-

terprife which was proposed; there seems to have been no obhl.
gation to engage in it imposed on him by public authority.
" When iny of the chief men proposes an expedition, such as ap-
prove of the cause and of the leader rife up, and declare their inten-
tion of following him; after coming under this engagement,
those who do not fulfil it, are confidered as deserters and traitors,
and are looked upon as infamous." Cef. ibid. c. 23. Tacitus
plainly points at the fame cuflom, though in terms more obscure.
Tacit. c. 1i. 4. As every individual was so independent, and
mailer in so great a degree of his own actions, it became of con-
sequence, the great objec of every person among the Germans,
who aimed at being a leader, to gain adherents and attach them
to his person and interest. These adherents Cefar calls Anbaai
and Cliete,, i. e. retainers or clients; Tacitus, Comiite, or com.
panions. The chief diftinaion and power of the leaders confited
in being attended by a numerous band of chosen youth. This
was their pride as well as ornament during peace and their de-
fence in war. The leaders gained or preserved the favour of
these retainers by presents of armour and of horses; or by the
profuse though inelegant hospitality with which they entertained
them. Tacit. c. 14, J1. $ . Another consequence of the personal
liberty and independence which the Genr.ans retained, even aftet
they united in society, was their circumscribing the criminal jurist
diAion of the magitirate within very narrow limits, and their not
only claiming but exercising almPft all the rights of private re-
fentment and revenge. Their magistrates had not the power
either of imprisoning or of infiiting any corporal puniihment of
a free man. Tacit. c. 7. Every person was obliged to avenge
the wrongs which his parents or friends had fullained. Their
enemies were hereditary, hut not irreconcilable. Even murder
was compensated by paying a certain number of cattle. Ta-
cit. c. 21. A part of the fine went to the king, or state a part
to the person who bad been injured, or to his kindred. Ibid. c.
Ia.

Those particulars concerning the institutions and manners of

the Germans, though well known to every person conversant
in ancient literature, I have thought proper to arrange in this or-
der, and to lay before such of my readers as may be less acquaint-
ed with these faes, both because they confirm the account which I
have given of the slate of the barbarous nations, and because they
tend to illustrate all the olervations 1 thall have occasion to make
concerning the various changes in their government and cultoms.
Tre laws and cuflems introduced b\ the barbarous nations intQ
th-ir new ftttlements, are the best commentary on the writings
of Cefar and Tacitus; and their observations are the best key to
a perfect knowledge of there laws and custom.s

One circumstance, with respect to the teltimonies of Cefarand

Tacitus, concerning the Ge-rnians, merits attention. Cafar
wrote his brief account of Their rmanners more than an hundred
y.ars before Tacitus composed his trt:tife De Moribus Germanr
urmni. An hundred years make a considerable period in the pro-

grefs of national manners, especially if, during that time, those
people who are rude and unpolifhed have had much communica-
tion with more civilized states. This was the cafe with the Ger-
mans. Their intercourse with the Romans began when Caesar
crofied the Rhine, and increased greatly during the interval be-
tween that event and the time when Tacitus flourilhed. We may
accordingly observe, that the manners of the Germans, in his
timr, which Caelar describes, were less improved than those of the
fame people as delineated by Tacitus. Besides this, it is remark-
able that there was a considerable difference in the state of society
among the different tribes of Germans. The Suiones were so
much improved, that they began to be corrupted. Tac. cap. 44.
The Fenni were so barbarous, that it is wonderful how they were
able to fubfifl. Ibid. cap. 46. Whoever undertakes to describe
the manners of the Germans, or to found any political theory up-
on the state of society among them, ought carefully to attend to
both these circumlRances.

Before I quit this fubje&, it may not be improper to observe,
that, though successive alterations in their institutions, together
with the gradual progress of refinement, have made an entire
change in the manners of the various people who conquered the
Roman empire, there is fill one race of men nearly in the fame
political ftuation with theirs, when thev first fettled in their new
onquelts; I mean the various tribes and nations of Savages in
North America. It cannot then be confidered either as a digref-
sinn, or as an improper indulgence of curiosity, to enquire whether
this similarity in their political state has occasioned any resemblance
between their character and manners. If the likeness turns out
to be flriking, it is a stronger proof that a just account has been
given of the ancient inhabitants of Europe, than the tetimony
even of Cefar or Tacitus.

i. The Americans Iubfift chiefly by hunting and fishing. Some

tribes neglec agriculture entirely. Among those who cultivate
some fmat spot near their huts, that, together with all works of
labour, is performed by the women. P. Charlevoix Journal Hil-
torique d'un Voyage de l'Amerique, 4to. Par. i744. p. .34. In
such a Rate of society, the common wants of men being few, and
their mutual dependence upon each other small, their union is ex-
tremely imperfe& and feeble, and they continue to enjoy their
natural liberty almost unimpaired. It is the first idea of an Ame-
rican, that every man is born free and independent, and that no
power on earth hath any right to diminish or circumscribe his na-
tural liberty. There is hardly any appearance of subordination
either in civil or domestic government. Every one does what he
pleases. Afather and mother live with their children, like persons
whom chance has brought together, and whom no common bond
unites. Their manner of educating their children is suitable to
this principle. They never chastise or punish them, even during
their infancy. As they advance in years, they continue to be en-
tirelv mailers of their own a6tions, and seem not to be conliiou3
of being responsible for any part of thefr conduc. Id. p. 272,

,77 --- 2. The power of their civil magistrates is extremely limit.

ed. Among most of their tribes, the Sachem or chief is elective.
A council of cld men is chosen to assist him, without whose advice
he determines no affair of importance. The Sachems neither
possess nor claim any great degree of authority. They propose
and intreat, rather than command. The obedience of their peo-
ple is altogether voluntary. Id. p. a66, z68-3. The savages
of America engage in their military enterprises, not from con-
fraint, but choice. When war is resolved, a chief arises, and of-
sers himself to he the leader. Such as are willing (for they com-
pel no pcrlbn) fiand up one after another, and sing their war song.
But if, after this, any of these ihould refute to follow the leader to
whom they have engaged, his life would be in danger, and he
would be confidered as the most infamous of men. Id. p. 217,
218.--4. Such as engage to follow any leader, expedt to be
treated by him with great attention and refped; and he is oblig-
ed to make them presents of considerable value. Id. p. 2 18.-
j. Among the Americans, the magifirate has scarcely any crimi-
nal jurifdiscion. Id. p. 272. Upon receiving any injury, the per
son or family offended may infiidt hat puniihment they pleae on
the person who was the author of it. Id. p. 274. Their resent.
nent and desire ofvengeance are excessive and implacable. Time
can neither extinguih nor abate it. It is the chief inheritance pa-
rents leave to their children; it is transmitted from generation to
generation, until an occasion be found of satisfying it. Id. p. 309.
Sometimes, however, the offended party is appealed. A compen-
fation is paid for a murder that has been committed. The rela.
tions of the deceased receive it; and it consists most commonly of
a captive taken in war, who, being substituted in place of the per.
son who was murdered, assumes his name, and is adopted into his
family. Id. p. 274. The resemblance holds in many other par.
ticulars. It is sufficient for my purpose to have pointed out the
fmilarity of those great features which distinguish and chara&er-
ize both people. Bochart, and other philologists of the last centu-
ry, who, with more erudition than science, endeavoured to trace
the migrations of various nations, and who were apt, upon the
flighteit appearance of resemblance, to find an affinity between na-
tions far removed from each other, and ti conclude that they were
descended from the fame ancestors, would hardly have failed, on
viewing such an amazing similarity, to pronounce with confidence
" That the Germans and Americans muff be the fame people."
But a philosopher will satisfy himself with observing, " That the
chara&ers of nations depend on the state of society in which they
live, and on the political inititutions eftabliihed among them; and
that the human mind, whenever it is placed in the fame situation
will, in ages the mofi dillant, and in countries the most remote,
afliume the fame form, and be diftinguilhed by the fame man
tiers."

I have pushed the comparison between the Germans and Ame

ricans no farther than was necessary for the illustration of my fiub
jea. I do not pretend that the state of focicty in the two coun

tries was perfecdly similar in every refpedt. Many of the German
tribes were more civilized than the Americans. Some of them
were not unacquainted with agriculture; almoif all of them had
fiocks of tame cattle, and depended upon them for the chief part
of their subsistence. Most of the American tribes subsist by hunt-
ing, and are in a ruder and more simple fiate than the ancient
Germans. The resemblance, however, between their condition,
is greater, perhaps, than any that hillory affords an opportunity of
observing between any two races of uncivilized people, and this
has produced a furpriing similarity of manners.

The booty gained by an army belonged to the army. The
king himrelf had no part of it but what he acquired by lot. A
remarkable instance of this occurs in the hiltory of the Franks.
The army of Clovis, the founder of the French monarchy, having
plundered a church, carried off, among other lacred utensils, a
vale of extraordinary size and beauty. The bishop sent deputies
to Clovis, beseeching him to restore the vase, that it might be a-
gain employed in the sacred lervices to which it had been confe-
crated. Clovis desired the deputies to follow him to Soissons, as
the booty was to be divided in that place, and promised, that if
the lot should give him the disposal of the vase, he would grant
what the bihop desired. When he came to Soissons, and all the
booty was placed in one great heap in the middle of the army,
Clovis entreated, that before making the division, they would give
him that vase over and above his Ihare. All appeared willing to
gratify the king, and to comply with his request, when a fierce and
haughty soldier lifted up his battle-axe, and firiking the vase with
the utmolt violence, cried out with a loud voice, " You ihall re-
ceive nothing here but that to which the lot gives you a right."
Gregor. Turon. Hilor. Francorum, lib. ii. c. 27. p. 70. Par. Ix6o.

The hiflory of the eftablifihnent and progress of the feudal fy-

I. While the barbarous nations remained in their original coun-
tries, their property in land was only temporary, and they had no

certain limits to their pofieffions. After feeding their flocks in one
great diftrid, they removed with them, and with their wives and
families, to another; and abandoned that likewise in a ihort time.
They were not, in confeqnence of this imperfecl species of proper.
ty, brought under any positive or formal obligation to serve the
community; all their services were purely voluntary. Every in.
dividnal was at liberty to choose how far he would contribute to.
wards carrying on any military enterprize. If he followed a leader
in any expedition, it was from attachment, not from a sense of o-
bligation. The clearest proof of this has been produced in Note
V1. While property continued in this fate, we can discover no-
thing that bears any resemblance to a feudal tenure, or to the sub.
ordination and military service which the feudal system introduced.

II. Upon fettling in the countries which they had subdued, the

vietorious troops divided the conquered lands. Whatever por-

his valour, as a settlement acquired by his own sword. He took
poffeffion-of it as a freeman in full property. He enjoyed it dur-

as an inheritance to his children. Thus property in land became
fixed. It was at the fame time allodial, i. e. the poffefTor had the

tion from the etymology of the words themfelyes. Alode or alto-
