obtained by lot. Wachteri Gloffar. Germanicun, voc. Alicdium
p. 5c. It appears from the authorities produced by him and by
Du Cange, voc. Sorn, that the northern nations divided the lands
which they had conquered in this manner. Feodum is com-
pounded of od poffEfion or eflate, and so wages, pay; intimating
that it was liperndary, and granted as a recompence for service.
Uachterus, ibid. voc. rodanm, p. 441.

The progress of the feudal system among the Germans was

perieclly fimlilar to that which we have traced in France. But as
the emperors of Germany, especially after the Imperial crown
ipafic' roti the dependents of Charlemagne to the house of Sax-
onv. were far fuptrior to the contemporary monarchs of France
in abilities, the Imperial vassals did not aspire so early to indepen-
denc. nor did they so soon obtain the privilege of possessing their
bel. . Ees by hereditary right. According to the compilers of the
Libi. Yeucorum, Conrad 11. or the Salic, was the firfi emperor
who rendered fiefs hereditary. Lib. i tit. i. Conrad began his

reign A. D. 1024. Ludovicus Pius, under whose reign grants
of hereditary fiefs were frequent in France, succeeded his father
A. D. 814. Not only was this innovation so much later in be-
ing introduced among the vassals of the German emperors, but e-
ven after Conrad had eitabliihed it, the law continued favourable
to the ancient practice; and unless the charter of the vassal bore
txprefsly that the fief descended to his heirs, it was presumed to
be granted only during life. Lib. feud. ibid. Even after the al-
teration made by Conrad, it was not uncommon in Germany to
grant fiefs only for life; a charter of this kind occurs as late as
the year 1376. Charta ap. Boehmer. Princip. Jur. feud. p. 36r.
The tranfriffion of fiefs to collateral and female heirs, took place
very slowly among the Germans. There is extant a clirter, A.
D. IzoI, conveying the right of fuccefiion to females, but it is
granted as an extraordinary mark of favour, and in reward of un-
common services. Boehmer ibid. p. 365. In Germany, as well
as in France and Italy, a confiderabie part of the lands continued
to be allodial long after the feudal mode of tenure was introduced.
It appears from the Codex Diplomaticus Monafterii Buch, that
a great part of the lands in the marquisate of Mifnia was still al-
lodial as late as the thirteenth century. No. I3, 36, 37, 46, &c.
ap. Scriptores hil. German. cura Schoetgenii & Kreyfigii. Al.
tenb. 1755. vol. ii. 183, &c. Allodial property seems to have
been common in another difirid of the fame province, during the
lame period. Reliquiae Diplomaticae Santimonial. Beutiz, Nc
17» 36, 58, ibid. 374, &c.

books either on parchment or on paper made of the Egyptian pa-
pyrus. SThe latter being the cheapest, was ofcourfe the most com-
monly used. But after the Saracens conquered Egypt in the fe-
venth century, the communication between that country and the
people fettled in Italy, or in other parts of Europe, was almoR en-
tirely broken off, and the papyrus was no longer in use among
them. They were obliged, on that account, to write all their
books upon parchment, and, as the price of that was high, books
became extremely rare and of great value. We may judge of the
frarcitv of the materials for writing them from one circumstance.
There 'ill remain several manuscripts of the eighth, ninth, and
following centuries, written on parchment, from which some for-
necr writing had been erased, in order to fubf'itute a new compo-
ition in its place. In this manner it is probable that several works
of the ancients perished. A book of Livy or of Tacitus might
be crazed, to make room for the legendary tale of a faint, or the
fuperflitious prayers of a missal. Murat. Antiq. Ital. v. iii. p. 833.
P. de Montfaucon affirms, that the greater part of the manuscripts
on parchment which he has seen, those of an ancient date except-
ed, are written on parchment from which some former treatise
had been crazed. Mem. de l'Acad. des Infcript. tom. ix. p. 315.
As the want of materials for writing is one reason why so many
of the works of the ancients have perilhed, it accounts likewise for
the small number of manuscripts of any kind, previous to the ele-
venth century, when they began to multiply from a cause which
Ihall bementioned. Hiftor. Liter. de France, tom. vi. p. 6. Ma-
ny circumstances prove the scarcity of books during these ages.

nafteries ofconfiderable note had only one missal. Murat. Antiq.

have parts of those books, there is no complete copy of them in all
France." Murat. Ant. v. iii. p. 835. The price of books became
so high, that persons of a moderate fortune could not afford to
purchase them. The countess of Anjou paid for a copy of the
Homilies of Haimon, bishop of Alberftadt, two hundred sheep, five

err, all its institutions and ceremonies must be immutable and ever-

times, those rites which were introduced during the ages of dark-
ness and credulity. What delighted and edified the latter, muf
dilguft and Thock the former. Many of the rites observed in the
Romish church appear manifestly to have been introduced by a
fupeiftition of the lowetl and most illiberal species. Many of
them were borrowed, with little variation, from the religious ce-
remonies eflablifhed among the ancient Heathens.- Some were fa
ridiculous, that if every age did not furniih instances of the fafci-
nating influence of superstition as well as of the whimsical forms
which it assumes, it must appear incredible that they should have
been ever received or tolerated. In several churches of France,
they celebrated a feitival in commemoration of the Virgin Mary's
flight into Egypt. It was called the feast of the Ass. A young
girl richly dressed, with a child in her arms, was set upon an als
iuperbly caparisoned. The ass was led to the altar in solemn pro-
cellion. High mass was said with great pomp. The ass was
tactht to kneel at proper praces; a hymn no legs ehidifh than
impious was sung in his praile; and when the ceremony was end-
ed, the priest, instead of the usual words with which he difiiffed
the people, brayed three times like an ass, and the people, initead
of the usual response, We bless the Lord, brayed three times in
the fame manner. Du Cange, voc. Fct,,,, v. iii. p. 424. This
ridiculous ceremony was not, like the feitival of fools, and some
orher pageants of those ages, a mere farcical entertainment exhi-
bited in a church, and mingled, as was then the cuflom, with an
imitation of rome religious rites; it was an aCt of devotion, per-
formed by the ministers of religion, and by the authority of the
church. However, as this pradtice did not prevail universally in
the Catholic church, its absurdity contributed at lat to abolilh
it.

Note XV. Se&. I. p. 35. [P].

The different steps taken by the cities of Italy in order to ex-
tend their power and dominions are remarkable. As soon as
their liberties were eftablifled, and they began to feel their own
importance, they endeavoured to render themselves marters of the
territory round their walls. Under the Romans, when cities en-
joved municipal privileges and jurifdiclion, the circumjacent lands
belonged to each town, and were the property of the community.
But as it was not the genius of the feudal policy to encourage ci-
ties, or to shew aly regard for their poffeflions and immunities,
these lands had been seized, and ihared among the conquerors.
The barons to whom they were granted, ere&ed their castles, al-
mofl at the gates of the city, and exercised theirjurifdidlion there.
Under pretence of recovering their ancient property, many of the
cities in Italy attacked these troublerome neighbours, and difpof-
tffing them, annexed their territories to the communities, and
made thereby a considerable addition to their power. Several
instances of this occur in the eleventh, and beginning of the
twelfth centuries. Murat. Antiq. ital. vol.iv. p. I59, &c. Their
ambition increasing together with their power, the cities after-
wards attacked feoeral barons situated at a greater diflance from
their walls, and obliged them to engage that they would become
members of their community; that they would take the oath of
L2i.: to their ma:iftrates; that they would fubje& their lands
to ail burdens and taxes imposed by common content; that they
would defend the community against all its enemies; and inha
they would reside within the city during a certain specified time
in each year. Murat. ibid. 163. This fubjetion of the nobility
to the municipal government established in cities, became almoit
universal, and was often extremely grievous to persons accustom-
ed to confider themselves as independent. Otto Frifingenfis thus
delcribes the fate of Italy under Frederick I. "The cities so
much assec liberty, and are so solicitous to avoid the insolence of
power, that almost all of them have thrown offevery other autho-
rity, and are governed by their own magistrates. Inforuch that
all that country is now filled with free cities, most of which have
compelled their biffops to reside within their walls, and there is
scarcely any nobleman, how great soever his power may be, who
is not lubjec to the laws and government of tome city." De Ge-
flis Frider. i. Imp. lib. ii.c. 13.p. 453. In another place he ob-
serves of the Marquis of Montserrat, that he was almost the only
Italian baron who had preserved his independence, and had not
become fubjeCd to the laws of any city. See also Muratori Anti-
chita Erenii, vol. i.p. 4I1, 411. That Rate into which some of
the nobles were compelled to enter, others embraced from choice.
They ohferved the high degree of security as well as of credit
and estimation, which the growing wealth and dominion of the
great communities procured to all the members of them. They
were desirous to partake of these, and to put themlelves under
such powerful protection. With this view they voluntarily be.
came citizens of the towns to which their lands were molt con.-

ticuous; and abandoning their ancient castles, took up their r.
fidence in the cities at least during part of the year. Several
deeds are still extant, by which some of the most illustrious fami:
lies in Italy are afloeiated as citizens of different cities. Murat
ib. p. I65, &c. A charter, by which Atto de Macerata is admit.
ted as a citizen of Ofmo, A. D. Ig98, in the Marcha di Ancona,
is Rill extant. In this he stipulates, that he will acknowledge
himfcif to be a burgers of that community; that he will to the
utnnoft of his power promote its honour and welfare; that he
will obey its maaiftrates; that he will enter into no leagues with
its enemies; that he will reside in the town during two months
in every year, or for a longer time, if required by the magistrates.
The community, on the other hand, take him, his family, and
friends, under their protecion, and engage to defend him a
gair.q every enemy. Pr. Ant. Zacharias Anecdota medii Evi.
Aug. Taur. I755. fol. p. 66. This privilege was deemed so im.
portant, that not only laymen, but ecclefiafics of the highest rank,
condescended to be adopted as members of the great communitis,
in hopes of enjoying the safety and dignity which that condition
conferred. Murat. ib. 179. Before the inrfitution of communi.
ties, per;ons of noble birth had no other residence but their caRtles
They kept their petty courts there; and the cities were deserted,
having hardly any inhabitants but Raves, or persons of low con-
dition. But in confequse .e of the praUtce which I have mt-.
tinned, cities not only became more populous, but were filled with
inhabitants of better rank, and a custom which fill subsists in Ita-
lv was then introduced, that all families of diftin6ion reside more
constantly in the great towns, than is usual in other parts of Eu.
rope. As cities acquired new consideration and dignity by the
accession of such citizens, they became more solicitous to preserve
their liberty and independence. The emperors, as sovereigns, had
anciently a palace in almost every great city of Italy; when they
visited that country they were accustomed to reside in these pa.
laces, and the troops which accompanied them were quartered
in the houses of the citizens. rhis the citizens deemed both igno-
minious and dangerous. They could not help confidering it a
receiving a master and an enemy within their walls. They la-
boured therefore to get tree of this fubjeclion. Some cities pre-
railed on the emperors to engage that they would never enter
their gates, but take up their reiidence without the walls: Chart.
Hen. IV. Murat. ib. p. 14. Others obtained the Imperial li-
cen:ce to puli down the palace situated within their liberties, on
condition that they build another in the suburbs for the occafiont
al reception of the emperor. Chart. Hen. IV. Murat. ib. p. IS.
These varions encroachments of the Italian cities alarmed the em-
perors, and put them on Ichemes for re-eflablilhing the Imperial
juriflicdion ove. them on its ancient footing. Frederick Barba-
roffa engaged in this enterprize with greatardour. The freec-
tie. of italy joined together in a general league, and flood on
their dclence: and after a long contest, carried on with alternate
fucceis, a loierm treaty of peace was concluded at ConItances

A. D. 1183, by which all the privileges and immunities granted
by former emperors to the principal cities in Italy were confirm-
edand ratified. Murat. Dissert. XLVIII. This treaty of Con-
stance was confidered as such an important article in the jurif-
prudence of the middle ages, that it is usually published together
sith the Libri Feudorum at the end of the Corpus Juris Civilis.
The treaty lecured privileges of great importance to the confede-
rate cities, and though it reserved a considerable degree of autho-
rity and jurisdiction to the empire, yet the cities persevered with
such vigour in their efforts in order to extend their immunities,
and the conjunedures in which they made them were so favour-
able, that, before the conclusion of the thirteenth century, moit
of the great cities in Italy had ihaken off all marks of fubje6tion to
the empire, and were become independent sovereign republics. It
is not requisite that I should trace the various fleps by which they
advanced to this high degree of power so fatal to the empire, and.
so beneficial to the cause of liberty in Italy. Muratori, with his u-
fual industry, has colkcted many original papers which illufirate
this curious and little known part of history. Murat. Antiq. Ital.
Diflert. L. See also Jo. Bapt. Villanovae Hist. Laudis Pompeii five
Lodi, in Graev. Thcf. Antiquit. Ital. vol. iii p. 888.

I. During that fiate of turbulence and disorder which the cor.

ruption of the feudal government introduced into Europe, person-
al safety was the first and great object of every individual; and
as the great military barons alone were able to give sufficient pro-
tection to their vassals, this was one great source of their power
and authority. But, by the institution of communities, effectual
provision was made for the safety of individuals, independent of
the nobles. For, z. The fundamental article in every charter
was, that all the members of the community, bound themfelve
by an oath to assist, defend, and stand by each other againfi all
aggressors, and that they should not susser any person to injure,
difirefi, or molest any of their fellow citizens. D'Acher. Spicel.
x. 641. xi. 741. &c.-z. Whover resided in any towni which
was made free, was obliged, under a severe penalty, to accede to
the community, and to take part in the mutual defence of its
members. D'Acher. Spic. xi. 344.--.1 The communities had
the privilege of carrying arms; of making war on their private
enemies; and of executing by military force any sentence which
their magifirates pronounced. D'Ach. Spicel. x. 643, 644. Xi
343-4. The practice of making fatisfadcion by a pecuniary
compensation for murder, afiault, or other acts of violence, mot
inconfifetnt with the order of society, and the safety of individuals,
was abolilhed; and such as committed these crimes were puniih
ed capitally, or with rigour adequate to their guilt. D'Ach. xi.
361. Mir'i Opera Diplomatica, i. 29z.-s. No member of a
community was bound to justify or defend himself by battle or
combat; but, if he was charged with any crime, he could be con-
victed only by the evidence of witnesses, and the regular course of
legal proceedings. Miraus,ibid. D'Ach. xi.3 75, 349. Ordon. tom.
iii. 265-6. If any man fufpeded himself to be in danger from
the malice or enmity of another, upon his making oath to that
effect before a magistrate, the person suspected was bound under
a fewere penalty to give surety for his peaceable behaviour,
D'Ach. xi. 346. This is the fame species of security which if
still know in Scotland under the name of Lovburrtwvs. In France,
it was firt introduced among the inhabitants of communities, and
having been found to contribute considerably towards pcrfonia

safety, it was extended to all the other members of focietv. Efta.
bliffcrnens de St Louis. liv. i. cap. 28. ap. Du Cange Vie de St.
Louils, p. 15.

II. The provisions in the charters of communities concerning
the tecurity of property, are not less considerable than those re-
fpesling personal safety. By the ancient law of France, no per-
son could be arreited or confined in prison on account of any pri-
vate deb. Ordon. des Rois de France, tom i. p. 7z- 80. If any
person was arrested upon any pretext, but his having been guilty
of a capital crime, it was lawful to rescue him out of the hands of
the officers who had seized him. Ordon. iii. p. 17. Freedom
from arrest on account of debt seems likewise to have been en-
joyed in other countries. Gudenus Sylloge Diplom. 47.1. In so-
ciety, while it remained in its rudest and most simple form, debt
seems to have been confidered as an obligation merely personal.
Men had made some progress towards refinement, before credi-
tors acquired a right of leizing the property of their debtors it
order to recover payment. The expedients for this purpose were
all introduced originally in communities, and we can trace the
gradual progress of them. x. The simplest and most obvious fpe-
cies of lecurity was, that the person who fold any commodity
should receive a pledge from him who bought it, which he re-

2. When no pledge was given, and the debtor became refradto-

strong hand, and by his private authority; the citizens of Paris

only to the violence of that which has been called a slate of na-

any society where laws and order were at a!i known. The or-

correts the law, and prohibits creditors fr.m seizing the effects
of their debtors, unless by a warrant from a magistrate, and under
his inspection, was not published until the year 13Sx. Ordon.
tom. ii. p. 438. It is probable, however, that men were taught,
by observing the disorders which the former mode of proceeding
occasioned, to corredt it in practice long before a remedy was pro-
vided by a law to that effetd. Every discerning reader will apply
this observation to many other cufioms and practices which I have
mentioned. New cufloms are not always to be ascribed to the

Soon after the introduction of the third estate into the national
council, the spirit of liberty which that excited in France began to
produce conspicuous effecs. In leveral provinces of France the no-
bility and communities formed associations, whereby they bound
thenirelves to defend their rights and privileges againfl the formi-

front towns into the States General. Hifloire de l'ancien Gou-
vernement de la France, tom. ii. p. 94. The vigour with which
the people aflerted and prepared to maintain their rights, obliged
their sovereigns to reCpeC them. Six years after this afiociation
Philip the Long issued a writ of summons to the community of
Narbonne, in the following terms: " Philip, by the grace, &c.
to our well-beloved, &c. " As we desire with all our heart, and
above all other things, to govern our kingdom and people in
peace and tranquillity, by the help of God; and to reform our
laid kingdom in so far as it ltands in need thereof, for the public
good, and for the benefit of our fuibje&ts, who in times past have
been aggrieved and oppressed in divers manners by the malice of
sundry perlbns, as we have learned by common report, as well
as by the information of good men worthy of credit, and we hav-
ing determined in-our counsel which we have called to meet in
our good city, &c. to give redress to the utmost of our power,
by all ways and means possible, according to reason and justice,
ntd willing that this should be done with solemnity and delibera-
tion by the advice of the prelates, barons, and good towns of our
rream, and particularly of you, and that it flould be tranfad&ed
agreeably to the will of God, and for the good of our people,
therefore we command," &c. Mably, Obfervat. ii. App. p. 386.
1 shall allow these to be only the formal words of a public and legal
fiyle; but the ideas are singular, and much more liberal and en-
arged than one could expect in that age. A popular monarch of

France a striking instance of the progress which the principles of

The regulations eftablifned by the States, held A. D. 1355, con.
cerning the mode of levying taxes, the administration of which
they vested not in the crown, but in commissioners appointed by
the States; concerning the coining of money; concerning the re.
dress of the grievance of purveyance; concerning the regular ad.
ministration of juflice; are much more suitable to the genius of
a republican government than that of a feudal monarchy. lhi
curious statute is published, Ordon. t. iii. p. 19. Such as have
not an opportunity to consult that large collekion, will find an
abridgen rnt of it in Hist. de France par Villaret, tom. ix. p. 130.
or in Hifloire de Boulainv. tom. ii. 2r3. The French historians
represent the bishop of Laon, and Marcel provost of the met.
chants of Paris, who had the chief direction of this assembly, as fe
ditious tribunes, violent, intercfled, ambitious, and aiming at in.
novatiens subversive of the constitution and government of their
country. That may have been the cafe, but these men poffiffed
the confidence of the people; and the measures which they pro.
posed as the most popular and acceptable, as well as molt likely
to increase their own influence, plainly prove that the spirit of li-
berty had spread wonderfuUly, and that the ideas which then pre-
vailed in France concerning government were extremely liberal,
The States General held at Paris, A. D. 1355, consisted of about
-eight hundred members, and above one half of these were depu-
ties from towns. ML Secoufe Pross. a Ordon. tom. iii. p. 48.
It appears that in all the different assemblies of the States, held
during the reign of John, the representatives of towns had great
influence, and in every refpe& the third State was confidered as
co-ordinate and equal to either bs the other two. Ibid. pallim.
These spirited efforts were made in France long before the house
of Coirmons in England acquired any considerable influence in
the legislature. As the feudal system was carried to its utmoll
height in France sooner than in England, so it began to decline
sooner in the former than in the latter kingdom. In England, al
most all attempts to establish or to extend the liberty of the people
have been successful; in France they have proved unfortunate.
What were the accidental events, or political causes, which occa.
Cioned tis diference, it is not my prelint business to enquire.

There is no custom in the middle ages more singular than that

of private war. It is a right of To great importance, and prevail.
ed so universally, that the regulations concerning it occupy a con-
fiderable place in the fvflem of laws during the middle ages. M.
de Montesquieu, who has unravelled so many intricate points in
feudal jurisprudence, and thrown light on so many customs for-
merly obscure and unintelligible, was not led by his fubje& to con-
fider this. I hall therefore give a more minute account of the cuf.
toms and regulations which direled a practice so contrary to the
present ideas of civilized nations concerning government and or-
der. 1. Among the ancient Germans, as well as other nations in
a similar slate of ociety, the right of avenging injuries was a pri.
rate and personal right cxercifed by force of arms, without any re-
ference to an umpire, or any appeal to a magistrate for decision.
The clearft proofs of this were produced, Note VI.--. This
pracice fubfifled among the barbarous nations after their fettle-
ment in the provinces of the empire which they conquered; and
as the cauSes of diffen6ion among them multiplied, their family
feuds and private wars became more frequent. Proofs of this oe-
cur in their early historians. Greg. Turon. Hill. lb. vii. c. 2. lib.
viii. c. i8. lib. x. c. 27. and likewise in the codes of their laws. It
was not only allowable for the relationsto avenge the injuries of
their family, but it was incumbent on them. Thus by the laws
of the Angli and Werini, ad quemcunque hereditas terre perve-
nerit, ad illum veflis bellica id eft lorica & ultio proximi, et folatio
leudis, debet pertinere, tit. vi. § 5. ap. Lindenbr. Leg. Saliq. tit.
63. Leg. Longob. lib. ii. tit. 14. § lo.-3. None but gentlemen,
or persons of noble birth, had the right of private war. All dif-
putes between slaves, villani, the inhabitants of towns, and free
men of inferior condition, were decided in the courts of justice.
All disputes between gentlemen and persons of inferior rank were
terminated in the fame manner. The right of private war fuppof-
ed nobility of birth, and equality of rank in both the contending
parties. Beaumanoir Costumes de Beauv. ch. lix. p. 300. Ordon.
des Rois de France, tom. ii. 395. § xvii. o08 § xv. &c. The dij
nified ecclefiafti-s likewise claimed and exercised the right of pri-
vate war; but as it was not altogether decent for them to prose-
cute quarrels in person, ad.scai or vidames were chosen by the fe
veral monatrries and bilhopric. These were commonly menof

high rank and reputation, who became the protectors of the ch urch-

rels, and fbught their battles; armis omnia quae erant ecclesia vi-
riliter defendebant, et vigilanter protegebant. Brullel Usage des

cafions, the martial ideas to which ecclesiastics of noble birth were

4 flamma, ferro, cede, possessiones ecclefiarum pralati defende-
bant." Guido Abbas ap. Du Cange, ib. p. I79.-4. It was not
every injury or trespass that gave a gentleman a title to make war
upon his adversary. Atrocious adts of violence, insults and as-
fronts publicly committed, were legal and permitted motives for
taking arms against the authors of them. Such crimes as are now
puniled capitally in civilized nations, at that time jutifled private
hostilities. Beauman. ch. lix. Du Cange Dissert. xxix. fur Join-

motive that could legally authorise a private war, yet disputes
concerning civil property often gave rife to hostilities, and were
terminated by the sword. Du Cange Diflert. p. 332.-5. All per-

committed, were included in the war which it occasioned; for it

Spoule the quarrel of the chieftain with whom they were connect-

ancient Germans, fulcipere tam inimicitias feu patris, feu propin-
qui, quam amicitias, neceffe eft;" a maxim natural to all rude na-
tions, among which the form of society, and political union,
firengthen flich a entiment. This obligation was enforced by le-
gal authority. If a person refused to take part in the quarrel of
is kinsman, and to aid him against his adversary, he was deemed
to have renounced all the rights and privileges of kindredihip, and
became incapable of succeeding to any of his relations, or of de-
riving any benefit from any civil right or property belonging to
them. Du Cange Dissert. p. 333. The method of ascertaining
the degree of affinity which obliged a person to take part in the
quarrel of a kinsman, was curious. While the church prohibited
the marriage of persons within the seventh degree of affinity, the
vengeance of private war extended as far as this absurd prohibi-
tion, and all who had such a remote connection with any of the
principals, were involved in the calamities of war. But when the
church relaxed somewhat of its rigour, and did not extend its pro-
hibition of marrying beyond the fourth degree of affinity, the fame
reftricion took place in the condu& of private war. Beauman.

in the kingdom which they conquered? It is asserted in an ordon-
nance of John king of France, that in all times past, persons of
every tank in Normandy have been prohibited to wage private
war, and the practice has been deemed unlawful. ' Ordon. tom.
ii. p. 407. If this fact were certain, it would go far towards ex-
plaining the peculiarity which I have mentioned. But as there
are some English Acts of Parliament, which, according to the re-
mark of the learned author of the ObfervationJ on bhe Statutes, cbiefly
tie more ancient, recite falsehoods, it may be added, that this is not
peculiar to the laws of that country. Notwithstanding the pofi-
tive assertion contained in this public law of France, there is good
reason for confidering it as a statute which recites a falsehood.
This, however, is not the place for discussing that point. It is
an inquiry not unworthy the curiosity of an Englilh antiquary.

In Germany, the disorders and calamities occasioned by the

right of private war were greater and more intolerable than in a-
ny other country of Europe. The Imperial authority was so
much fiaken and erfeetbled by the violence of the civil wars ex-
cited by the contests between the popes and the emperors of the
Franconian and Suabian lines, that not only the nobility but the
cities acquired almost independent power, and scorned all fubor-
dination and obedience to the laws. The frequency of thefefaidz,
or private wars, is often mentioned in tle German Annals, and
the fatal effects of them are most pathetically described, Datt de
Pace Imper. pub. lib. i. cap. v. No. 30, & passim. The Germans
early adopted the qTreuga Dei, which was first eflablilhed in
France. This, however, proved but a temporary and intffe&u-
al remedy. The dilolucir multiplied so fail, and grew to be so
enormous, that they threatened the dissolution of society, and
compelled the Germans to have recourse to the only remedy of
the evil, viz. an ahfolute prohibition of private wars. The em-
peror William publilhed his edil to this purpose, A. D. 1255,
an hundred and sixty years previous to the ordonnance of
Charles VI. in France. Datt, lib. i. cap. 4. No. 20. But neither
he nor his successors had authority to lecure the observance of it.
This gave rife to a practice in Germany, which conveys to us a
striking idea bo:h of the intolerable calamities occasioned by pri-
vate wars, and of the feeblenels of government during the twelfth
and thirteenth centuries. The cities and nobkls entercd into

alliances and aiabciations, by which they bound themselves
to maintain the public peace, and to make war on such as
should violate it. This was the origin of the league of the Rhines
of Suabia, and of many smaller confederacies diltinguifhed by va-
rious names. The rife, progress, and beneficial effects of thece
aflbciations are traced by Datt, with great accuracy. Whatever
degree of public peace or of regular administration was preserved
in the empire from the beginning of the twelfth century to the
close of the fifteenth, Germany owes to these leagues. During
that period, political order, refped for the laws, together with the
equal adminiltration of justice, made considerable progress in Ger-
many. But the final and perpetual abolition of the right of pri-
vate war was not accomplished until A. D. 1495. The Imperial
authority was by that time more firmly established, the ideas of
men with respect to government and subordination were become
more just. That barbarous and pernicious privilege of waging
war, which the nobles had so long possessed, was declared to be
incompatible with the happiness and exiitence of society. In or-
der to terminate any differences which might arise among the
various members of the Germanic body, the imperial chamber
was instituted with supreme jurifdidion, to judge without appeal
in every question brought before it. That court has subsisted
since that period, forming a very refpedable tribunal, of eflential
importance in the Germanic constitution. Datt, lib. iii. iv. v.
Pfeffel Abrege de l'Hitoire, du Droit, &c. p. 556.

The text contains the great outlines which mark the course of

private and public jurifdiition in the several nations of Europe. I
thall here follow more minutely the various fReps of this progress,
as the matter is curious and important enough to merit this atten-
tion. The payment of a fine by way of fatisfacion to the person
or family injured, was the first device of a rude people, in order to
ch-eck the career of private resentment, and to extinguish thofefai-
dr, or deadly feuds, which were prosecuted among them with the
uimoft violence. This cufiom may be traced back to the ancient
Germans. Tacit. de Mrorib. Germ. c. z2. and prevailed among
other unc;vilized nations. Many examples of this are colleted by
the ingenious and learned author of Historical Law TraAs, vol. i.
p. 41. These fines were alcertained and levied in three different
manners. At first they were fettled by voluntary agreement be-
tween the parties at variance. When their rage began to fubfidet
and they felt the bad efe&ts of their continuing in enmity, they
came to terms of concord, and the fatisfacdion made was called a
eswpV;tiit, implying that it was fixed by mutual coufent. De l'E-

sprit des I,oix, lib. xxx. c. 19. It is apparent from some of the
more ancient codes of laws, that at the time when these were
comrpiled, matters still remained in that simple state. In certain
chfs, the person who had committed an offence was left exposed
to the resentment of those whom he had injured, until he ihould
recover their favour, quoquo modo potuerit. Lex Frifion. tit. Ix.
g i. The next mode of levying these fines was by the sentence
of a-biters. An arbiter is called in the Regiam Majeftatem ami-
cabilis compositor, lib. xi. c. 4. § lo. He cou d eltimate the de-
gree of offence with more impartiality than the parties interested,
and determine with greater equity what fatisfation ought to be
deranded. It is difficult to bring an authentic pr.of of a cuflom
previous to the records preserved in any nation of Europe. But

arbiter wanted authority to enforce his decisions, judges were ap-

in their decisions. Previous to this last ftep, the expedient ol pay-
ing compofitinns w'as an imperfect remedy againit the. pernicious
efflet. of private resentment. As soon as this important 'hange
was introduced, the magiflrate, putting himself in place of the per-

reft satisfied. Every possible injury that could occur in the inter-
course of civil society was confidered and estimated, and the com-

ed against the violence of resentment. The enacding of this was
a conliderable ftep towards improvement in criminal jurifpru-
dence. In some of the more ancient codes of laws, thej/rea are
ailogether omitted, or Ib seldom mentioned, that it is evident they
were but little known. In the later codes, the cJedum is as precise-
I: specified as the composition. In common cafes it was equal to

their fubieAion to civil courts. This privilege, like their other

796, to which M. I'Abbe de Soy refers in his Notice de Di-

fuperflitious respeCt of the laity for the clerical character and

lie grants them judicium non tantum fanguinolentis plage, fed

barous nations, but were governed entirely by the Roman law.

They regulated all their tranfacions by such of its maximin
were preserved by tradition, or were contained in the Theodosian
code, and other books extant among them. This we learn from
a custom which prevailed universally in those ages. Every per.
son was permitted to choose among the various codes of laws
then in force, that to which he was willing to.conform. In any
tranfa*ion of importance, it was usual for the persons contramting
to mention the law to which they submitted, that it might be
known how any controversy that ihould arise between them was
to be decided. Innumerable proofs of this occur in the charten
of the middle ages. But the clergy confidered it as such a valu.
able privilege of their order to be governed by the Roman lav,
that when any person entered into holy orders, it was usual for
him to renounce the code of laws to which he had been formerly
subjed, and to declare that he now submitted to the Roman law.

The code of the canon law began to be compiled early in the

NOTE XXV. SeE. I. p. 65. [BB].

The rapidity with which the knowledge and study of the Ro-
man law spread over Europe is amazing. The copy of the Pan-
decs was found at Amalphi, A. D. 1137. Irnerius opened a
college of civil law at Bologna a few years after. Giann. Hist.
book xi. c. 2. It began to be taught as a part of academical
learning in different parts of France before the middle of the cen-
tury. Vaccarius gave lectures on the civil law at Oxford, as ear-
ly as the year 1147. A regular system of feudal law, formed
plainly in imitation of the Roman code, was composed by two
Milanese lawyers about the year zI0o. Grarian published the
code of canon law, with large additions and emendations, about
the fame time. The earliest collection of those customs, which
served as the rules of decision in the courts ofjuftice, is the A.fffct
de Jerusalem. They were compiled, as. the preamble informs us,
in the year Io99, and are called Jus Confuetudinarium quo rege-
batur regnum orientale. Willerm. Tyr. lib. xix. c. . But e-
culiar circumflances gave occasion to this early compilation. The
vitorious Crusaders fettled as a colony in a foreign country, and
adventurers from all the different nations of Europe composed
this new society. It was neceflary on that account to ascertain
the laws and customs which were to regulate the tranfadtions of
business, and the administration of jultice among them, But in
no country of Europe was there, at that time, any collecdion of
customs, nor had any attempt been made to render law fixed.
The sirs undertaking of that kind was by Glanville, Lord Chief
Jultice of England, in his Tra6tatns de Legibus & Confuetudini-
bus Anglie, composed about the year xi8g. The Regiam Ma-
jeftatem in Scotland, ascribed to David I. seems to be an imitation,
and a servile one, of Glanville. Several Scottilh antiquaries, un-
der the influence of that pious credulity, which disposes men to
afient, without hesitation, to whatever they deem for the honour
of their native country, contend zealoutly, that the Regiam Ma-
jellatem is a production prior to the treatise of Glanville; and
have brought themselves to believe, that a nation, in a superior
fate of improvement, borrowed its laws and institutions from
one considerably less advanced in its political progress. The in-
ternal evidence (were it my province to examine it) by which this
theory might be refuted, is, in my opinion, decilive. The exter-
nal circumstances which have seduced Scottih authors into this
niftake, have been explained with so much precision and candour
by Sir David Dilrymple, in lis examination of some of the argu-
Vol. I. X

nments for the high antiquity of Regiam Majeftatem, Edin. z769,

But the laws of the middle ages afford a proof fill more con-

Paefi Bassi, p. ro8, iS8. The sugar cane was introduced into
the Wefl Indies before that time, but the cultivation of it.was not

quence in commerce. In the middle ages, though sugar was not

Sates.

These varios commodities with which the Italians fwurnii

he 'other nations of Europe, procured them a favourable recep-
tion in every kingdom. They were established in France in the
thirteenth century with most extensive immunities. They not
only obtained every indulgence favourable to their commerce,
but personal rights and privileges were granted to them, which
the natives of the kingdom did not enjoy. Ordon. tom. iv. p.
668. By a special proviso, they were exempted from the Droit
D'Aubaine. Ibid. p. 670. As the Lombards (a name frequently
given to all Italian merchants in many parts of Europe) engrolfed
the trade of every kingdom in which they fettled, they became maf-
ters of its cath. Money of course was in their hands not only a sign
of the value of other commodities, but became an obje& of com-
merce itself. They dealt largely as bankers. In an ordonnance,
A. D. r295, we find them tiled wmrcatores and camnporel. They
carried on this as well as other branches of their commerce with
somewhat of that rapacious spirit which is natural to monopoliz-
ers who are not refrained by the competition of rival traders.
An absurd opinion, which prevailed in the middle ages, was,
however in some measure, the cause of their exorbitant demands,
and may be pleaded in apology for them. Trade cannot be car-
ried on with advantage, unless the persons who lend a fum of
money are allowed a certain premium for the use of it, as a comn
penfation for the rifl which they run in permitting another to
traffic with their flock. This premium is fixed by law in all
commercial countries, and is called the legal interest of money.
But the Fathers of the church had preposterously applied the
prohibitions of usury in ifripture to the payment of legal interest,
and condemned it as a fin. The fchoolmen, miffled by Aristotle,
whose Sentiments they followed implicitly, and without examina-
tion, adopted the fame error, and enforced it. Blackilone's
Commentaries on the Laws of England, vol. ii. p. 455. Thus
the Lombards found themselves engaged in a traffic which was
tvery where deemed criminal and odious. They were liable to
punishment if detected. They were not satisfied, therefore, with
that moderate premium, which they might have claimed if their
trade had been open and authoriled by law. They exacted a
fum proportional to the danger and infamy of a discovery. Ac-
cordingly, we find that it was usual for them to demand twenty
per cent, for the use of money in the thirteenth century. Murat.
Antiq. Ital. vol. i. p. 893. About the beginning of that century,
the countess of Slanders was obliged to borrow money in order
to pay her huxband's ranfor. She procured the fum requisite,
either from Italian merchants or from Jews. The lowest interest
which the paid to them was above twenty per cent. and some of
them exacted near thirty. Martene and Durand. Thefaur. An-
ecdotorum, vol. i. p. 886. In the fourteenth century, A. D.
13 1, Philip IV. fixed tle interef which might be legally exa&-
td in the fairs Champagne at twenty per cent. Ordonan. tom. i.
P. 484. The interest of money in Aragon was somewhat lower.
James I. A. D. 1242, fixed it by law at eighteen per cent. Petr.
4t Marca. .Murfa five Limes Hilpan. app. 1433. As late as the

year 1490, it appears that the interest of money in Placentia was
at the rate of forty per cent. This is the more extraordinary, be.
cause at that time the commerce of the Italian states was become
considerable. Memoire Storiche de Piacenza, tom. viii. p. 1o4.
Piac. 1760. It appears from Lud. Guicciardini, that Charles V.
had fixed the rate of interest in his dominions in the Low Coun.
tries at twelve per cent. and at the time when he wrote, about the
year i56o, it was not uncommon to exact more than that funs.
le complains of this as exorbitant, and points out its bad effess

The high interest of money is alone a proof that the profits on

§s87. The Hanseatic league is the most powerful commercial confede-

In England, the progress of commerce was extremely flow; and
the causes of this are obvious. During the Saxon heptarchy, Eng-
land, i-plit into many petty kingdoms, which were perpetually at
variance with each other; exposed to the fierce incurlioin of the
Danes, and other northern pirates, and funk in barbarity and ig-
norance, was in no condition to cultivate commerce, or to pursue
any system of useful and salutary policy. When a better profpet
began to open by the union of the kingdom under one monarch,
the Norman conquest took place. This occasioned such a violent
fIock, as well as such a sudden and total revolution of propertv,
that the nation did not recover from it during several reigns. 1;y
the time that the conflitution began to acquire some Itability, and
the Englilh had so incorporated with their conquerors as to be-
come one people, the nation engaged with no less ardour than
imprudence in support of the pretenlions of their sovereigns to the
crown of France, and long wafted its vigour and genius in its
wiid efforts to conquer that kingdom. When by its ill fulccefs,
and ..eatcd disappointments, a period was at raft put to this fa-
tai pl. nzy, and the nation beginning to enjoy Ibme rcpole, had
leifurc to breathe and to gather new strength, the destructive wars
between the houses of York and Lancaster broke out, and involv-

td the kir.gdom in the worfit of all calamities. Thus, besides the
common obftruaions of commerce occasioned by the nature of
the feudal government, and the state of manners during the mid.
dle ages, its progress in England was retarded by peculiar causes
Such a fuccefflon of events adverse to the commercial spirit wau
sufficient to have checked its growth, although every other circum.
fiance had favoured it. The Engliih were accordingly one of the
afi nations in Europe who availed themselves of those commercial

This union did not resemble the confederacies in other feudal

templates the humanity of his countrymen. He compares the
laws of Aragon to those of Rome, as both exempted citizens and
freemen from such ignominious and cruel treatment, and had re-
course to it only in the trial of slaves. Zurita had reason to be-
flow such an encomium on the laws of his country. Torture was
at that time permitted by the laws of every other nation in Eu-
rope. Even in England, from which the mild spirit of legislation
has long baniihed it, torture was not, at that time, unknown. Ob-
fcrvations on the Statutes, chiefly the more ancient, &c. p. 66.

The other fad shows, that the fame spirit which influenced the

legislature prevailed among the people. In the year 1483, the re-
ligious zeal of Ferdinand and Isabella prompted them to intro-
duce the inquisition into Aragon. 'I'hough the Aragonefe were
no less fuperltitioufly attached than the other Spaniards to the Ro-
man Catholic faith, and no less desirous to root out the feeds of
error and of heresy which the Jews and Moors had scattered, yet
they took arms against the inquisitors, murdered the chief inqui-
fitor, and long opposed the establishment of that tribunal. The
realon which they gave for their conduct was, That the mode of
trial in the inquisition was inconfittent with liberty. The crimi-
nal was not confronted with the witnesses, he was not acquaint-
ed with what they deposed againt him, he was fubjetded to torture,
and the goods of persons condemned were confiscated. Zurita
Analcs, torn. iv. p. 341.

The form of government in the kingdom of Valencia, an&

principality of Catalonia, which were annexed to the crown of
Aragon, was likewise extremely favourable to liberty. The Va.
lencians enjoyed the privilege of union in the fame manner with
the Aragonefe. But they had no magistrate resembling thejufti.
za. The Catalonians were no less jealous of their liberties than
the two other nations, and no less bold in aflerting them. But it
is not necessary for illufirating the following hiflory to enter into
any farther detail concerning the peculiarities in the conflitutioa
of thefr kingdoms.

NOTE XXXVI. Sea . p. 144. [NN].

The military order of St. Jago, the most honourable and opu-

lent of the three Spanish orders, was instituted about the year
1170. The bull of confirmation by Alexander III. is dated A.D.
1176. At that time a considerable part of Spain fiill remained
under fubjetion to the Moors, and the whole country was much
exposed to depredations not only of the enemy, but of banditti
It is no wonder then, that an institution, the objec of which was
to oppose the enemies of the Christian hith, and to restrain and
punirh those who disturbed the public peace, should be extremely
popular, and meet with general encouragement. The wealth
and power of the order became so great, that according to one
historian the grand rmarer of St. Jago was the person in Spain of
greatest power and dignity next to the king. .l1. Anton. Ne-
.rirfcnfis, ap. Schott. Scrip. Hifp. i. 8r2. Another historian ob-
serves, that the order pofcfled every thing in Caltile that a king
would most desire to obtain. 'Lurita Anaics, v. 22. The knights
took the vows of obedience, of poverty, and of conjugal chastity.
By the former they were bound implicitly to obey the commands
of their grand master. The order could bring into the field a
thousand men at arms. ,E1. Ant. Nebref. p. 813. If, as we
hare reason to believe, there men at arms were accompanied, as
was usual in that age, this was a formidable body -of cavalry.
There belonged to this order eighty-four comnmanderies, and two
hundred priories and other benefices. Dissertations fur la Cheva-

lrie par Hon. de St. Marie, p. z62. It is obvious how formida-
ble to his sovereign the command of these troops, the adminifira-
tion of such revenues, and the disposal of so many offices, must
have rendered a subjed. The other tJo orders, though inferior
to that of St. Jago in power and wealth, were nevertheless very
considerable fraternities. When the conquest of Granada de-
prived the knights of St. Jago of those enemies againit whom
their zeal was originally directed, fuperflition found out a new
object, in defence of which they engaged to employ their cou.
rage. To their usual oath, they added the following clause:
" We do swear to believe, to maintain, and to contend in public
and in private, that the Virgin Mary, the Mother of God, our
Lady, was conceived without the stain of original fin. "-This ad-
dition was made about the middle of the seventeenth century.
lIonore de St. Marie Dissertations, &c. p. 263.-Nor is such a
fingilar engagement peculiar to the order of St. Jago. The mem-
bers of the second military order in Spain, that of Calatrava, e-
qually zealous to employ their prowess in defence of the honours
of the Blessed Virgin, have likewise professed themselves her true
knights. Their vow, conceived in terms more theologically ac-
curate than that of St. Jago, may afford some amusement to an
Englilh reader. i' I vow to God, to the Grand Mailer, and to
you who here represent his person, that now, and for ever, I will
maintain and contend, that the Virgin Mary, Mother of God,
our Lady, was conceived withput original fin, and never incurred
the pollution of it; but that in the moment of her happy concep-
tion, and of the union of her foul with her body, the Divine Grace
prevented and preserved her from original guilt, by the merits
of the passion and death of Christ our Redeemer, her future son,
foreseen in the Divine Council, by which ihe was truly redeemed,
and by a more noble kind of redemption than any of the children
of Adam. In the belief of this truth, and in maintaining the ho-
nour of the most Holy Virgin, through the strength of Almighty
God, I will live and will die." Definiciones de la Orden de Ca-
latrava, conforme al Capitulo General en 1652, fol. Madr. 1748.
p. 153. Though the church of Rome hath prudently avoided to
give its farkion to the dodirine of the immaculate conception,
and the two great monastic orders of St. Dominick and St. Fran.
cis have espoused opposite opinions concerning it, the Spaniards
are such ardent champions for the honour of the Virgin, that
when the present king of Spain inflituted a new military order in
the year 1771, in commemoration of the birth of his grandson,
he put it under the immediate proteCtion of the most Holy Mary
in the mystery of her immaculate conception. Conflitutiones de
la Real y diftinguida Orden. Efpanola de Carlos III. p. 7. To
undertake the defence of the Virgin Mary's honour, had such a
resemblance to that (pecies of refined gallantry, which was the
original object of chivalry, that the zeal with which the military
orders bound themselves, by a solemn vow, to defend it, was
worthy of a true knight in those ages, when the spirit of the in-
ititution subsisted in full vigour. But in the present age, it must

excite some surprise to fee the inflitution of an illustrious order
connected with a doctrine so extravagant and destitute of any
foundation in scripture.

NOTE XXXVIIL Se&. III.p. 48. [PP].

Nothing is more common among antiquaries, and there is not
a more copious oIurce of error, than to decide concerning the in-
flitutions and manners of past ages, by the forms and ideas which
prevail in their own times. The French lawyers in the seven-
teentll and eighteenth centuries, having found their sovereigns in
polreffion of ahfolute power, seem to think it a duty incumbent
on thenl to maintain that such unbounded authority belonged to
the crown in every period of their monarchy. c' The govern-
ment of France," fays M. de Real very gravely, " is purely mo-
narchical at this day, as it was from the beginning. Our kings
were absolute originally as they are at present." Science du Go-
veruement, tom, i. p. 3 1. It is impossible, however, to conceive
two flates of civil society more unlike to each other, than that of
the French nation under Clovis, and that under Lewis XV. It
is evident from the codes of laws of the various tribes which set-
tled in Gaul and the countries adjacent to it, as well as from the
history of Gregory of Tours, and othei early annalists, that a-
mong all these people the form of government was extremely
rude and simple, and that they had scarcely begun to acquire the
first rudiments of that order and police which are necelfary in ex-
tensive societies. The king or leader had the command of fol-
diers or companions, who followed his standard from choice, not
by constraint. I have produced the clearest evidence of this,
Note VI. An event related by Gregory of Tours, lib iv. c. 14.
affords the most striking proof of the dependence of the early
French kings on the sentiment and inclination of their people.
Clotaire I. having marched at the head of his army, in the year
55 , against the Saxons, that people, intimidated at his approach,
sued for peace, ahd offered to pay a large fum to the offended
monarch. Clotaire was willing to close with what they propos-
ed. But his army insisted to be led forth to battle. The king
employed all his eloquence to persuade them to accept of what
the Saxons were ready to pay. The Saxons, in order to soothe
them, increased their original offer. The king renewed his soli-
citations: but the army, enraged, ruffed upon the king, tore his
tent in pieces, dragged him out of it,- and would have slain him
on the spot, if he had not consented to lead them instantly against
the enemy.

If the early monarchs of France possessed such limited authori-

ty, even while at the head of their army, their prerogative during
peace will be found to be till more confined. They ascended the '
throne not by any hereditary right, but in consequence of the e-
lesion of their fubjets. In order to avoid an unnecessary num-
ber of quotations, I refer my readers to Hottomanni Francogallia,
cap. vi. p. 47. edit. 1573, where they will find the fullest proof
of this from Gregory of Tours, Amoinus, and the most authen-
tic historians of the Merovingian kings. The effet of this elec-
tion was not to invest them with absolute power. Whatever re -
lated to the general welfare of the nation, was submitted to pu.
blic deliberation, and determined by the suffrage of the peoples

NOTE XXXIX. Sect. m.p. 149. [QQ.

That important change which the constitution of France un-

derwent, when the legislative power was transferred from the
great council of the nation to the king, has been explained by the
French antiquaries with less care than they bestow in illuitrating
other events in their history. For that reason I have endeavour-
ed with grea'er attention to trace the feps which led to this me-
morable revolution. I shall here add some particulars which tend
to throw additional light upon it. The Leges Salicae, the Leges
Burgundion;il,, and other codes publihed by the several tribes
v-hich ietted in Gaul, were general laws extending to every per-
son, to every province and diltria where the authority of those
tribWes wa ack.nowl dged. But they seem to have become obfo-
kte; and the reason of their falling into disuse is very obvious.
Almost the whole property of the nation was allodial when these
la-xs were framed. But when the feudal institutions became gene-
rl, and gave rife to an infinite variety of questions peculiar to that
fpc;ie= oi tenure, the ancient codes were of no use in deciding

with regard to there, because they could not contain regulations
applicable to cafes which did not exist at the time when they were
comipiled. This considerable change in the nature of property
made it necessary to publilh the new regulations contained in the
Capilularia. Many of these, as is evident from the perusal of them
were public laws extending to the whole French nation, in the ge-
neral aflhmbly of which they were enacted. The. weakness of
the greater part of the monarchs of the second race, and the difg
order into which the nation was thrown by the depredations of
the Normans, encouraged the barons to usurp an independent
power formerly unknown in France. The nature and extent of
that jurifditdion which they assumed I have formerly conlidered.
The political union of the kingdom was at an end, its ancient
conflitution was dissolved, and only a feudal relation fubffited be-
tween the king and his vaflals. The regal jurifdicdion extended
no further than the domains of the crown. Under the last kings
of the second race, these were reduced almost to nothing. Under
the first kings of the third race, they comprehended little more
than the patrimonial estate of Hugh Capet, which he annexed to
the crown. Even with this accession, they continued to be of
finall extent. 'Valley, Hist. de France, tom. iii. p. 32. Many of
the most considerable provincesin France did not at first acknow-
ledge Hugh Capet as a lawful monarch. There are till extant
leveral charters, granted during the first years of his reign, with
this remarkable clause in the form of dating the charter; " Deo
rcgnante, rege expedfante," regnante domino nofitro Jesu Chrifto,
Francis autem contra jus regnum ufurpante Ugone rege. Bou-
quet Recueil, tom. x. p. 544. A monarch whose title was thus
openly disputed, was not in a condition to assert the royal jurif-
diurion, or to limit that of the barons.

All thele circumfiances rendered it easy for the barons to u-
furp the rights of royalty within their own territories. The Ca-
pitularia became no less obsolete than the ancient laws; and
customs were every where introduced, and became the sole rule
by which all civil tranfadtions were condu6ted, and all caules
were tried. The wonderful ignorance, which became general
in France during the ninth and tenth centuries contributed to the
introdution of customary law. Few persons, except ecclelia-
Itics, could read; and as it was not in the power of such illiterate
persons to have recourse to written laws, either as their guide
in business, or their rule in administering justice, the customary .
law, the knowledge of which was preserved by tradition, univer-
fall) prevailed.

During this period, the general afrembly of the nation seems
not to have been called, nor to have once exerted its legislative
authority. Local customs regulated and decided every thing.
A flriking proof'of this occurs in tracing the progress of the
French jurisprudence. The last of the Capitularia collected by
M. Baluze, was issued in the year 921, by Charles the Simple.
Au hundred and thirty years elapsed from that period to the pu-
blication of the firtt ordonnance of the kings of the third race,

contained in the great collecAion of M. Lauriere, and the first
ordonnance which appears to be an at of legislation extending
to the whole kingdom, is that of Philip Auguflus, A. D. r190.
Ordon. tom. i. p. i. I8. During that long period of two hun.
dred and fixty-nine years, all tranfations were directed by local
customs, and no addition was made to the statutory law ofFrance.
The ordonnances, previous to the reign of Philip Auguflus con-
tain regulations, the authority of which did not extend beyond
the king's domains.

Various inflances occur of the caution with which the kings

of France ventured at first to exercise legislative authority. M.
I'Ah. de Mahly produces an ordonoance of Philip Augufltus, A.
D. o206, concerning the Jews, who, in that age, were in some
measure the property of the lord in whose territories they resided.
But it is rather a treaty of the king with the countess of Cham-
pagne, and the compte de Dampierre, than an ac of royal pow-
er; and the regulations in it seem to be established not so much
by his authority, as by their consent. Obfervat. fur I'Hift de
France, ii. p. 355. In the fame manner an ordonnance of Louis
ViI!. concerning the Jews, A. D. 12z3, is a contrad between
the king and his nobles, with refpecd to their manner of treating
that unhappy race of men. Ordon. tom. i. p. 47. The Eftab~
licfmens of St. Louis, though well adapted to serve as general
laws to the whole kingdom, were not publiled as such, but only
as a complete code of cuflomary law, to be of authority within
the king s domains. The wifdom,the equity, and the order con-
fpicuoius in that code of St. Louis, procured it a favourable re-
ception throughout the kingdom. The veneration due to the
virtues and good intentions of its author, contributed not a little
to reconcile the nation to that legislative authority which the
king began to assume. Soon after the reign of St. Louis, the idea
of the king's possessing supreme legislative power became com-
mon. If, fays Beaumanoir, the king makes any establishment
especially for his own domain, the barons may nevertheless ad-
here to their ancient eufloms; but if the establishment be gene-
ral, it Ihall be current throughout the whole kingdom, and we
ought to believe that such eflablifhments are made with mature
deliberation, and for the general good. Count de Beauvoifis, c.
48. p. 265. Though the kings ofthe third race did not call tihe
general aflembly of the nation, during the long period from
Hugh Capet to Philip the Fair, yet they seem to have consulted
the bishops and barons who happened to be present in their court,
with refptct to any new law which they published. Examples of
this occur, Ocdon. tom. i. p. 3 & 5. This practice seems to have
continued as late as the reign of St. Louis, when the legislative
authority of the crown was well eflablifhed. Ordon. tom. ;. p.
58. A. D. 1246. This attention paid to the barons faciliated the
kings acquiring such full poffleion of the legislative power, as
enabled them afterwards to exercise it without observing that for-
rmality.

The aflemblies diflinguihed by the name of the States Gencra

vere firfl called, A. D. 1302, and were held occasionally from
that period to the year 1614, since which time they have not
been summoned. These were very different from the ancient
afilcmblies of the French nation under the kings of the first and
second race. There is no point with refpedt to which the French
antiquaries are more generally agreed, than in maintaining that
the States General had no suffrage in the passing of laws, and
poflrffed no proper legislative jurisdiction. The whole tenor of
the French hiflory confirms this opinion. The form of proceed-
ing in the States General was this:-The king addressed himself,
at opening the meeting, to the whole body assembled in one
place, and laid before them the affairs on account of which he
had summoned them. Then the deputies of each of the three
orders, of nobles, of clergy, and of the third estate, met apart,
and prepared their cabier or memorial, containing their answer to
the propositions which had been made to them, together with
the representations which they thought proper to lay before the
king. These answers and representations were confidered by the
king in his council, and generally gave rife to an ordonnance.
'I hele ordonnances were not addressed to the three estates in
common. Sometimes the king addressed an ordonnance to each
of the estates in particular. Sometimes he mentioned the assem-
bly of the three estates. Sometimes he mentioned the assem-
bly of that estate to which the ordonnance is addrefled. Some-
times no-mention at all is made of the assembly of estates, which
suggested the propriety of enadling the law. Preface au tom. ii.
des Ordon. p. xx. Thus the States General had only the privi-
lege of advising and remonfirating; the legislative authority ro.
tided in the king alone.

Iiuit theretore fcarch for ibme other source of this high privilege.
'Ilie parliament was originally composed of the mofi eminent

or legislative authority. It was natural, therefore, during the in-

tain its approbation and concurrence, before any ordonnance was
publilhed, to which the people were required to conform. s.
Under the second race of kings, every new law was reduced in-
to proper form by the chancellor of the kingdom, was proposed
by him to the people, and when enacted, was committed to him
to be kept among the public records, that he might give authentic
copies of it to all who should demand them. Hincm. de Ord. Pa.
lat.c. 16. Capitul. Car. Calv. tit. xiv. § Ir. tit. xxxiii. The

naverunt. Bouquet Recueil, tom. v. p. 663. Malllw signifies not

by the Comes, or missus dominicus. Sea6ini were the judges, or
the alrffiors of the judges in that court. Here then seems to be a
very early instance, not only of laws being published in a court of
julice, but of their being verified or confirmed by the subscription

troduced the verifying of edicts in the parliament of Paris. But
this conj-lture I propose with that diffidence, which I have felt in
all my reasonings concerning the laws and inflitutions of foreign
nations. 3. This fupireme court ofjuftice in France was dignified
with the appellation of parliament, the name by which the gene-
ral assembly of the nation was diftinguifed towards the close of
the second race of kings; and men, both i: reasoning and in con-
dut, are wonderfully influenced by the finilarity of names. 'he
prcferving the ancient names of the magifirates eftablilhed while
the republican government fuhfilled in Rome, enabled Augulus
and his fucccffci s to aflume new powers with less observation and
greater cafe. The bcltowing the fame name in France upon two
courts, which were extremely different, contributed not a little to
confound their -uri:diclion and funocions.

All these circumstances concurred in leading the kings of France
to avail themfelvcs of the parliament of Paris, as the infirument
of reconciling the people to the exercise of legislative authority by
the crown. 'The French, accustomed to fee all new laws examin-
ed and authorised before they were pubiilhed, did not sufficiently
diflinguifh between the effec of performing this in the national
assembly, or in a court appointed by the king. But as that court
was composed of refpetable members, and who were well Ikilled
in the laws of their country, when any new edidt received its fanc-
tion, that was sufficient to dilpofe the people to submit to it.

When the pracdice of vcrifying and reg/'-ring the royal ediets in

the parliament of Paris became common, the parliament contend-
ed that this was necessary in order to give them legal authority.
It was eflablifhed as a fundamental maxim in French'jurifprudence,
that no law could be puhlifhed in any other manner; that with-
out this formality no edict or ordonnance could have any effet;
that the people were not bound to obey it, and ought not to con-
fider it as an edid or ordonnance until it was verified in the fu-
preme court, after free deliberation. Roche-flavin des Parlemens
de France, 4to. Gen. 1621. p. 92I. The parliament, at different
times, hath, with great fortitude and integrity, opposed the will
of their sovereigns; and, notwithflanding repeated and perempto-
ry requisitions and commands of the crown, hath refused to veri-
fr and publifl such edids as it conceived to be opprflfive to the
people, or subversive of the conflitution of the kingdom. Roche-
fiavin reckons, that between the year 156z and the year 1589, the
parliament refused to verify more than a hundred ediits of the
kings. Ibid. 925. Many instances of the spirit and conilancy
with which the parliaments of France opposed pernicious laws,
and asserted their own privileges, are enumerated by Limnaeus in
his Notitim Regni Francie, lib. i. c, 9. p. 224.

But the power of the parliament to maintain and defend this
privilege, bore no proportion to its importance, or to the courage
with which the members afierted it. When any monarch was de-
termined that an edi&t Should be carried into execution, and found
the parliament inflexibly resolved not to verify or publish it, he
could easily fnpply this defet by the plenitude of his regal power.
ie repaired to the parliament in person, he took poifeiion of his
frat of jusice, and commanded the edict to be read, verified, re-
gifered, and publihed in his presence. Then, according to an-
other maxim of French law, the king himself being present, nei-
ther the parliament, nor any magistrate whatever, can exercise
any authority, or perform any function. Adveniente Principe,
cfhat magiflratus. Roche-flavin, ibid. p. 928, 929. Encyclopt-
die, ton. ix. Art. Lit. de -uiscr, p. 58i. Roche.flavin mentions
fcveral initances of kings who actually exerted this prerogative, so
fatal to the residue of the rights and liberties tranfrnittcd to the
Irench by their ancestors. Pafquier produces tome instances of
tle fame kind. Rech. p. 6l. Limneus enumerates many other
iiltances, but the length to which this note has swelled prevents
'it from inserting them at length, though they tend greatly to i!-

lustrate this important article in the French history, p. 445.' Thus
by an exertion of prerogative, which, though violent;feems to be
constitutional, and is justified by innumerable precedents, all the
effrts of the parliament to limit and control the king's legislative
authority are rendered ineffectual.

I have not attempted to explain the conflitution orjurifdicion of

any parliament in France but that of Paris. All of them are form-
ed upon the model of that most ancient and refpecable tribunal,
and all my observations concerning it will apply with full force to
them.

As I have endeavoured in the history to trace the various steps

I. 'With refpec to the power, jurifdition, and revenue of the

a dignitary in each chapteror religious house. The right of grant-

We find that in all the ancient elections to which I have refer-

This was the first origin of the exclusive right which the elecdors

they were unwilling to waste them to no purpose. The rights of
the seven eleclors were supported by all the descendents and allies

Abrege, p. 376, The seven electors were confidered as the re-
presentatives of all the orders which composed the highest claa

of the three great diftricAs into which the empire was anciently di-
vided; one king, one duke, one marquis, and one count. All
there circumstances contributed to render the introduction of this
